Commonwealth v. Sepulveda


No. 553 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


618 Pa. 262; 55 A.3d 1108; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 2808


July 25, 2008, Submitted 
November 28, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal From the Order entered on October 11, 2007 denying PCRA relief in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Monroe County at No. CP-45-CR-0001522-2001. Trial Court Judge: Ronald E. Vican, President Judge.
Commonwealth v. Sepulveda, 579 Pa. 217, 855 A.2d 783, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 1923 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The supreme court considered appellant's capital appeal from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania) denying his petition for relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9541-9546. Appellant was convicted of two counts of first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court found no error in the PCRA court's finding that there was no obvious objective indication to counsel, state police, or the trial court that appellant suffered from any mental illness at the time he confessed his crimes, such that the police conduct could be viewed as unconstitutional manipulation warranting suppression. The supreme court had recognized that a defendant convicted of multiple murders had a difficult task in establishing his claim that he was prejudiced by counsel's failure to adequately investigate and present evidence in mitigation. In instances where the result concerning prejudice was not self-evident, but instead required careful analysis of prejudice in the specific factual context of the case, the supreme court had remanded for the PCRA court to conduct the prejudice inquiry. As the evidence of the experts offered at the PCRA hearing established the possibility of additional mitigating circumstances, the supreme court concluded that the better course was to have the PCRA court conduct the prejudice inquiry assisted by relevant advocacy from both sides. Accordingly, remand was required for an appropriate Strickland inquiry into prejudice.


OUTCOME: The order of the PCRA court was affirmed insofar as it dismissed all claims other than that of ineffective assistance of counsel associated with the investigation, development, and presentation of mitigation evidence. With respect to this claim, the PCRA court's order was vacated, and the matter was remanded for further consideration consistent with the opinion. Jurisdiction was relinquished.

Commonwealth v. Gehris


No. 10 WAP 2011


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


618 Pa. 104; 54 A.3d 862; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 3024


October 18, 2011, Argued 
September 27, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of Superior Court entered March 26, 2010 at No. 1960 WDA 2008, affirming the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County entered August 27, 2008 at CP-10-CR-0000477-2007. Trial Court Judge: William R. Shaffer, Judge.
Commonwealth v. Gehris, 52 A.3d 223, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 2247 (Pa., 2012)
Commonwealth v. Gehris, 996 A.2d 541, 2010 Pa. Super. LEXIS 517 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed his sentence imposing the lifetime registration provisions of Megan's Law, 42 Pa.C.S. § 9795.1(b), following his guilty plea to three charges of criminal solicitation to commit sexual abuse of a child and a single attempt to corrupt a minor charge.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, appellant asserted that because the two or more enumerated offenses committed were within the same episode, such a harsh penalty was unwarranted. The evenly divided state supreme court found that the registration requirements of Megan's Law were not intended to be penal in nature. The primary purpose of the registration requirement was not to punish an individual convicted of sexual offenses, but, instead, registration was mandated for the protection of the public. Appellant entered a guilty plea, thereby admitting that he committed the act of soliciting the undercover state police officer posing as a 19-year-old female to obtain nude photographs of a person whom he believed to be a 13-year-old child, and also that he committed the act of soliciting the undercover state police officer posing as the 19-year-old female to procure the 13-year-old child so that he could engage in sexual activity with the child, which was deemed to be sexual exploitation. Thus, the clear terms of 42 Pa.C.S. § 9795.1(b)(1) required appellant to register with the Pennsylvania State Police for life because he had two convictions for offenses specifically listed in 42 Pa.C.S. § 9795.1(a).


OUTCOME: Because the state supreme court was evenly divided, the order of the superior court was affirmed.

Applewhite v. Commonwealth


No. 71 MAP 2012

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant law opponents (LOs) sought review of a single-judge order from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which denied their application for a preliminary injunction (PI) in their petition for review, challenging the constitutional validity of Act 18 of 2012, also known as the Voter ID Law and codified at 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2626(b).

The Law contemplated that a driver's license or the non-driver equivalent under § 1510(b) of the Vehicle Code, 75 Pa.C.S. § 1510(b), would primarily be used. It was acknowledged that the Law was not being implemented according to its terms, and that some registered voters would be unable to comply with the DOT's requirements for obtaining a card under § 1510(b). The LOs asserted a facial constitutional challenge to the Law under Pa. Const. art. 1, § 5 and sought to preliminarily enjoin its implementation. The Commonwealth Court denied relief, noting that other methods to obtain a valid ID card were being expeditiously implemented. On appeal, the court found that the Commonwealth Court had to assess the actual availability of the alternate ID cards to determine whether there would be voter disenfranchisement for purposes of the upcoming election that required a PI.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the Commonwealth Court and returned the matter to the Commonwealth Court for further proceedings.

Commonwealth v. Mouzon


No. 9 EAP 2011


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


617 Pa. 527; 53 A.3d 738; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 1889


September 15, 2011, Argued 
August 21, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court entered on July 22, 2009, at No. 2609 EDA 2009 (reargument denied on September 15, 2010) vacating and remanding the Judgment of Sentence entered on April 23, 2009 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. CP-51-CR-0011769-2007. Appeal allowed February 16, 2011 at 564 EAL 2010. Trial Court Judge: Teresa M. Sarmina, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Jacqueline O. Shogan, Robert E. Colville, Robert A. Freedberg, JJ.
Commonwealth v. Mouzon, 6 A.3d 569, 2010 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3801 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth appealed the judgment of the Pennsylvania Superior Court, which vacated appellee's conviction for first-degree murder, aggravated assault, carrying a firearm without a license, and possessing an instrument of crime. Appellee had been convicted following a jury trial and was sentenced to a mandatory term of life imprisonment for murder and an aggregate concurrent term of 10 to 20 years of imprisonment on the remaining charges.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was at a bar and harassed two women that had rebuked his advance. The victim witnessed appellee's treatment of the women and intervened. The pair began fighting. It was undisputed that the victim punched appellee multiple times before appellee pulled his gun and shot the victim in the head. Appellee asserted self defense but never testified at his trial. The Superior Court reversed appellee's convictions upon concluding that the trial court erred in excluding evidence of the victim's prior criminal record. The Court determined that the Superior Court erred in concluding that there was adequate evidence of self-defense presented at trial to render the victim's prior record potentially admissible because appellee never provided any subjective testimony on what he believed to justify using deadly force. Further, the court held that the Superior Court erred in finding that the trial court improperly excluded evidence of the victim's prior conviction because such evidence was not relevant to the questions of whether appellee was free from fault. The Court held that the exclusion of such evidence was not a proper basis for overturning the murder conviction.


OUTCOME: The Court reversed the Superior Court and reinstated the judgment of sentence for first-degree murder, aggravated assault and related offenses.

Pa. State Educ. Ass'n ex rel. Wilson v. Pa. Office of Open Records

April 10, 2012, Argued 
August 21, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Motion granted by, Motion denied by Pa. State Educ. Ass'n v. Commonwealth, 2013 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 926 (2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 396 MD 2009 dated September 24, 2010. Other Court Judge: Bonnie Brigance Leadbetter, President Judge, Bernard L. McGinley, Dan Pellegrini, Mary Hannah Leavitt, Patricia A. McCullough, Johnny J Butler, Kevin P. Brobson, JJ.
Pa. State Educ. Ass'n ex rel. Wilson v. Pa. Office of Open Records, 4 A.3d 1156, 2010 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 522 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, individual school employees from multiple school districts and the Pennsylvania State Education Association, an organization whose membership consisted of teachers and education support professionals, sought review of the Commonwealth Court's (Pennsylvania) jurisdiction where the school employees sought injunctive and declaratory relief against appellee Office of Open Records (OOR) as to their home addresses.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth Court granted two petitions to intervene and sustained the OOR's preliminary objections, finding that it lacked jurisdiction because the employees had not named an indispensable Commonwealth party as a defendant. The employees argued that the Commonwealth Court erred by refusing to exercise its original jurisdiction over their claims for declaratory and injunctive relief where numerous Right-to-Know Law (RTKL), 65 Pa. Plainly, the Right-to-Know Law (RTKL), as presently implemented by the OOR, did not provide public school employees with a reliable administrative or judicial method by which to seek redress for action that they believe violated the statutory scheme and/or their constitutional rights; thus, it was just and proper for the OOR to be haled into court to address core and colorable issues connected with such treatment at the behest of affected persons and their associations as justice could not be done in the OOR's absence.


OUTCOME: The decision of the Commonwealth Court was vacated, and the matter was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the appellate court opinion. Jurisdiction was relinquished.

Commonwealth v. Spotz


No. 610 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


616 Pa. 164; 47 A.3d 63; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 1412


August 29, 2011, Submitted 
June 26, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order entered on March 25, 2010, in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of York County at No. CP-67-CR-0001673-1995. Trial Court Judge: John S. Kennedy, Judge.
Commonwealth v. Spotz, 562 Pa. 498, 756 A.2d 1139, 2000 Pa. LEXIS 2039 (Aug. 22, 2000)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was convicted in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of York County (Pennsylvania) of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. After his motion for postconviction relief was denied, appellant filed an appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued, inter alia, that counsel were ineffective under U.S. Const. amend. VI for failing to investigate or develop a defense of diminished capacity due to voluntary intoxication or mental defect. The supreme court disagreed. The only evidence cited by appellant that supported the viability of a defense grounded in diminished capacity due to mental defect was one excerpt of the testimony of a psychiatrist who evaluated appellant years after the murders. Appellant failed to recognize that the evidence presented at trial strongly indicated that, at the time of the victim's murder, appellant was able to formulate the intent to evade the authorities, who he knew were seeking him in connection with a prior homicide; to steal a car from the victim and drive to a remote area with her as a passenger; to take her cash, as well as her credit cards and rings, which he attempted to sell; and to drive away rapidly in her vehicle after firing three shots. All of that evidence of appellant's directed, intentional, goal-oriented activity at or near the time of the murder argued strongly against his assertion that diminished capacity would have been a viable guilt-phase defense.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Keaton


No. 418 CAP, No. 419 CAP, No. 420 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


615 Pa. 675; 45 A.3d 1050; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 1267


March 24, 2005, Submitted 
May 30, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Commonwealth v. Keaton, 623 Pa. 229, 82 A.3d 419, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 3029 (Dec. 17, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order entered on 3-10-2003 in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Philadelphia County *denying PCRA relief for a new trial at Nos. 1925-1935 March Term 1993. *Court of Common Pleas Granted a new penalty phase hearing but not a new trial. Trial Court Judge: Carolyn Engel Temin, Senior Judge.
Commonwealth v. Keaton, 556 Pa. 442, 729 A.2d 529, 1999 Pa. LEXIS 1015 (Apr. 12, 1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate appealed a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Philadelphia County denying guilt phase relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9541-9546. Appellee Commonwealth cross-appealed the grant of a new penalty phase.


OVERVIEW: The inmate's postconviction motion dealt with three cases, two rape cases and a murder case. On appeal, the inmate claimed, inter alia, that his right to counsel under the Fifth and Sixth Amendments and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9 was violated when he was questioned about an earlier rape case and the murder case while he was in custody for the second rape case, which was brought first. The supreme court concluded a remand was necessary, because the parties disagreed over whether the right was invoked and there was no clear answer in the record. While the inmate remained silent and counsel was appointed, there was nothing indicating that he specifically invoked the right to counsel. The supreme court found merit in any other errors claimed by the parties. Among other things, the supreme court noted that although the inmate's intelligence scores and age of onset met the definition of mental retardation, the third element was not established, as the record only revealed limitation in one area, academics and thus, the inmate was subject to the death penalty. However, the award of a new penalty phase was proper, as counsel failed to investigate and present mitigating mental health evidence.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded for a determination of whether the inmate invoked his Fifth Amendment right to counsel in relation to the first action. The judgment of the PCRA court was otherwise affirmed.

Mesivtah Eitz Chaim of Bobov, Inc. v. Pike County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 16 MAP 2011


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


615 Pa. 463; 44 A.3d 3; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 964

April 25, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the order of Commonwealth Court at No. 2343 CD 2008 dated December 29, 2009 affirming the order of the Pike County Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, at No. 1095-1997 dated September 11, 2008. Trial Court Judge: Joseph F. Kameen, President Judge.
Mesivtah Eitz Chaim of Bobov, Inc. v. Pike County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 985 A.2d 310, 2009 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1731 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a not-for-profit religious entity, challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order of a trial court that upheld appellee county board of assessment's denial of appellant's real estate tax exemption request, pursuant to Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 2(a)(v). Intervenor school district was also a party to the appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellant operated a summer camp in the county and sought a property tax exemption as a purely public charity. Appellee denied the exemption request, and the Commonwealth Court affirmed, reasoning that occasional use of appellant's recreational and dining facilities by county residents was insufficient to prove it relieved the county's government of its burden as required by the case law test entitled HUP. Appellant argued on appeal that the Pennsylvania Legislature's enactment of criteria in the Institutions of Purely Public Charity Act (Act 55), 10 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 371-385, for determining if an organization qualifies as a purely public charity under Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 2 was deserving of deference in deciding whether an organization qualifies. The Court disagreed and held that to receive a real estate tax exemption without violating the Pennsylvania Constitution, the party must meet the definition of a purely public charity as measured by the test in HUP. If it did so, it may qualify for the exemption if it meets the statute's requirements. Act 55, however, cannot excuse the constitutional minimum: if you do not qualify under the HUP test, you never get to the statute.


OUTCOME: The Court affirmed the order of the Commonwealth Court.

Allegheny County Deputy Sheriffs' Ass'n v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd.


No. 29 WAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


615 Pa. 126; 41 A.3d 839; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 635; 193 L.R.R.M. 2724


April 12, 2011, Argued 
March 26, 2012, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Decision reached on appeal by Allegheny County Deputy Sheriffs' Ass'n v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd., 68 A.3d 6, 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 129 (Pa. Commw. Ct., May 2, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered January 28, 2010, at No. 959 CD 2009, affirming the Order of the Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board entered April 21, 2009, at No. PF-R-08-74-W. 990 A.2d 86 (Pa.Cmwlth. 2010). Intermediate Court Judges: Robert E. Simpson, Judge, Mary Hannah Leavitt, Judge, James R. Kelley, Judge.
Allegheny County Deputy Sheriffs' Ass'n v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd., 990 A.2d 86, 2010 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 25 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association brought a proceeding seeking review of a determination by appellee labor board which dismissed the association's petition for representation of deputy sheriffs under 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 217.1-217.10 (Act 11). The Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirmed. The association appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether deputy sheriffs of counties of the second class were "police officers" for purposes of collective bargaining under Act 111. The trial court found that they were not, determining that the deputies had failed to demonstrate that they could meet a two-pronged test. The appellate court found, however, that the controlling factor was that the general assembly, in two pieces of legislation, specifically singled out for definition as police officers, deputy sheriffs of counties of the second class. Deputy sheriffs of counties of the second class had been specifically designated by the general assembly as police officers in the Crimes Code and the Municipal Police Education and Training Law, 18 Pa.C.S. § 103 and 53 Pa.C.S. § 2162. Deputy sheriffs of counties of the second class were police officers for purposes of Act 111. Thus, the labor board's and the trial court's application of a judicially and administratively created test to examine this issue was erroneous. Nothing in 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1101.805, relied upon in part by the trial court, compelled, or even suggested, a different result.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order was vacated, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

112 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


In re Stevenson


No. 54 MAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


615 Pa. 50; 40 A.3d 1212; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 631


September 8, 2010, Submitted 
March 26, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Order of Commonwealth Court dated August 19, 2010 at No. 643 MD 2010 which Granted the petition to strike name from Ballot. Other Court Judge: James R. Kelley, Senior Judge.
In re Nomination Petitions & Papers of Stevenson, 608 Pa. 397, 12 A.3d 273, 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2274 (2010)



DISPOSITION: Residual jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Objectors filed a petition to set aside a candidate's nomination paper on grounds that signatures thereon were invalid. After reversal and remand, the Pennsylvania Commonwealth Court ordered the Secretary of the Commonwealth to strike the candidate's name from the ballot. The candidate did not appeal, but filed an application asking the high court determine whether the statutory district residency requirement violated U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: The objectors had argued that the nomination paper was invalid because the circulator of those pages resided outside the legislative district, in violation of § 2911(d) of the Pennsylvania Election Code, 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2600-3591. The candidate relied on Morrill v. Weaver, in which a federal district court had held that § 2911(d) violated the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I. The trial court had ruled that § 2911(d) was valid and set the nomination paper aside. The high court had reversed and remanded the case with instructions to decide it on nonconstitutional grounds; it reserved jurisdiction over the constitutional issue. On appeal, the candidate renewed his First Amendment argument. The high court held that while this issue was moot, since the candidate had not appealed the trial court's order on remand, it addressed it because of its public importance and potential to recur. Morrill, which had permanently enjoined the Commonwealth from enforcing § 2911(d) on grounds it violated the First Amendment, had preclusive effect because the Commonwealth had fully litigated the matter and had not appealed; therefore, § 2911(d) was unenforceable, absent legislative amendment.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the judgment of the trial court, held that the district residency requirement discerned in § 2911(d) was unenforceable by the Secretary and the courts of Pennsylvania, and relinquished residual jurisdiction.

Commonwealth v. Wilgus


No. 6 MAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


615 Pa. 32; 40 A.3d 1201; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 637


October 20, 2010, Argued 
March 26, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court at No. 1100 MDA 2008, dated 06/26/2009, affirming the Order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County at No. CP-22-CR-0002439-2007, dated 05/27/2008. Trial Court Judge: Scott A. Evans, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Cheryl Lynn Allen, Judge; John M. Cleland, Judge; James J. Fitzgerald, III, Justice.
Commonwealth v. Wilgus, 2009 PA Super 116, 975 A.2d 1183, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1769 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)



DISPOSITION: Order reversed; case remanded for proceedings consistent with this decision. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth appealed an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) affirming the trial court's dismissal of charges against defendant for violation of Megan's Law requirements in 18 Pa.C.S. § 4915(a)(1) and (2).


OVERVIEW: The issue before the supreme court was whether the lower courts erred by concluding that a defendant who provides an address and subsequently becomes homeless has no duty to comply with the registration requirements of Megan's Law, such that evidence consistent with these circumstances would be insufficient to support a verdict of guilty of failure to register. The Commonwealth argued that the superior court erred in finding the evidence insufficient to conclude defendant had a "residence" as defined in Megan's Law. Defendant argued he was not in violation of Megan's Law because it required registration of a residence, which he did not have because he was homeless. The supreme court concluded that the lower courts erred, holding that defendant's obligation to notify police was triggered when he changed his previous residence, not when his new residence was established. The supreme court noted that there was no exception for homeless offenders and the superior court was incorrect in reading such an exception into the statute.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the superior court was affirmed.

 

114 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Fagan v. Smith


No. 41 MM 2012


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


615 Pa. 87; 41 A.3d 816; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 453



February 29, 2012, Decided 
February 29, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Petition for Writ of Mandamus or, Alternatively, Application for the Exercise of Original Jurisdiction under King's Bench Power and Emergency Relief.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, voters in the enumerated districts, filed suit against respondents, the Speaker of the Pennsylvania House of Representatives (HOR) and the Secretary of the Commonwealth, seeking, inter alia, a request for mandamus relief in order to compel the Speaker to issue writs of elections for special elections in order to fill vacancies in the HOR.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the voters met their burden with respect to their mandamus request. They showed that they had a clear legal right to elected representation in the HOR districts. Further, the right had to be vindicated at special elections due to vacancies of certain seats pursuant to Pa. Const. art I, § 5. The court also noted that the Speaker of the HOR, as its presiding officer, had the corresponding duty to issue writs of election for special elections to fill those vacancies pursuant to Pa. Const. art II, § 2. It was noted that the duty was mandatory, ministerial, and nondiscretionary. The court concluded that there was no apparent reason why the constitutionally mandated, ministerial duty to issue writs of election to fill the vacant seats should be deferred pending the outcome of the reapportionment process under Pa. Const. art. II, § 17. The discretion conferred in the limited exception under 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2778a did not justify the Speaker's failure to abide by his non-discretionary ministerial duty.


OUTCOME: The court granted the voters' request for it to exercise its King's Bench jurisdiction. It granted the request for mandamus relief in part and ordered the Speaker of the HOR to issue writs of election for special elections to fill the vacancies in the HOR.

Daley v. A.W. Chesterton, Inc.


No. 27 EAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


614 Pa. 335; 37 A.3d 1175; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 352; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P18,792


December 1, 2010, Argued 
February 21, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on April 15, 2009 at No. 2763 EDA 2006 vacating the Order entered on September 12, 2006 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, at No. 2640, October Term 2005, 971 A.2d 1258 (Pa. Super. 2009). Trial Court Judge: Paul P. Panepinto, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: John L. Musmanno, Judge, Susan Peikes Gantman, Judge, Jack A. Panella, Judge.
Daley v. A.W. Chesterton, Inc., 2009 PA Super 71, 971 A.2d 1258, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 86 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant manufacturers challenged the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed a trial court's grant of summary judgment in their favor. Appellees, a worker and his wife, filed a personal injury action after the worker was diagnosed with malignant pleural mesothelioma. In 1990, appellees had previously filed an action against several defendants seeking compensatory damages for work-related pulmonary asbestosis and lung cancer.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted appellants summary judgment after acknowledging that the Pennsylvania Superior Court in Marinari adopted, for purposes of asbestos litigation, a two-disease, or separate disease, rule, allowing a plaintiff to bring one action based on a nonmalignant asbestos disease and a subsequent action for any separately diagnosed malignant disease, but finding that the two-disease rule permitted a plaintiff to bring only one cause of action for nonmalignant diseases caused by asbestos exposure. Since appellees had previously filed an action for the malignant disease of lung cancer, the trial court held that they were precluded from bringing a subsequent action for the malignant disease of mesothelioma. The Superior Court reversed, finding that the separate disease rule allowed an individual to bring separate lawsuits for more than one malignant disease which allegedly resulted from the same asbestos exposure. The Court agreed, holding that a plaintiff who is diagnosed with a malignant disease, and later diagnosed with a separate and distinct malignant disease caused by the same asbestos exposure, may benefit from the separate disease rule.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the Superior Court and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Holt v. 2011 Legislative Reapportionment Comm'n


No. 7 MM 2012, No. 1 WM 2012, No. 2 MM 2012, No. 3 MM 2012, No. 4 MM 2012, No. 5 MM 2012, No. 6 MM 2012, No. 8 MM 2012, No. 9 MM 2012, No. 10 MM 2012, No. 17 MM 2012, No. 4 WM 2012


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


614 Pa. 364; 38 A.3d 711; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 232


January 23, 2012, Argued, Submitted; January 24, 2012, Submitted 
January 25, 2012, Decided 
February 3, 2012, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at, Injunction denied by, Motion denied by Pileggi v. Aichele, 843 F. Supp. 2d 584, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 15227 (E.D. Pa., 2012)
Reaffirmed, Appeal dismissed by Holt v. 2011 Legislative Reapportionment Comm'n, 67 A.3d 1211, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 923 (Pa., May 8, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Legislative Reapportionment Plan of the 2011 Legislative Reapportionment Commission, dated December 12, 2011. [***2]
Holt v. 2011 Legislative Reapportionment Comm'n, 614 Pa. 364, 38 A.3d 711, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 186 (Pa., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In twelve separate matters, appellant citizens, acting singly or in groups, filed appeals from the Final Plan for legislative redistricting of Pennsylvania, which was devised by appellee, the 2011 Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission (the LRC), in response to the U.S. decennial census.


OVERVIEW: While the court ordered accelerated briefing and oral argument, the LRC failed to adopt a Final Plan in a timeframe that offered the remote prospect of appellate review before the primary season began. The LRC's inexplicable delay ensured that primary candidates who relied upon the Final Plan did so at their peril. The delay of the LRC in producing a Final Plan created a situation where the election primary season was disrupted. In any event, the citizens demonstrated that the Final Plan, considered as a whole, contained numerous political subdivision splits that were not absolutely necessary, and the Plan thus violated the constitutional command with respect to the integrity of political subdivisions found in Pa. Const. art. II, § 16. Furthermore, the citizens showed that the LRC could have easily achieved a substantially greater fidelity to all of the mandates in art. II, § 16, including compactness, contiguity, and integrity of political subdivisions, yet the LRC did not do so in the Final Plan. Accordingly, an order was issued under Pa. Const. art. II, § 17(d) declaring the Plan contrary to law, and remand to the LRC for reapportionment was necessary.


OUTCOME: In a per curiam order, the court declared that the Final Plan was unconstitutional. It remanded the matter to the LRC to reapportion the Commonwealth in a manner consistent with its opinion.

Commonwealth v. Miller


No. 58 MAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


613 Pa. 584; 35 A.3d 1206; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 148


May 11, 2011, Argued 
January 20, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court at No. 1199 EDA 2007 dated November 23, 2009 Vacating the judgment of sentence and Remanding the Order of the Delaware Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, at No. CP-23-CR-0004026, dated April 17, 2007. Appeal Allowed September 29, 2010 at 991 MAL 2009. Trial Court Judge: Barry C. Dozor, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Richard B Klein, Susan Peikes Gantman, Cheryl Lynn Allen, JJ.
Commonwealth v. Miller, 988 A.2d 725, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5791 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth appealed the order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which vacated the judgment of sentence convicting appellee of second-degree murder, in violation of 18 Pa.C.S. § 2502.


OVERVIEW: Following a jury trial, appellee was found guilty of second-degree murder, theft by unlawful taking, and fleeing or attempting to elude an officer, but he was acquitted of first-degree murder, robbery, aggravated assault, and possessing an instrument of crime. The jury rendered no verdict on third-degree murder and theft by receiving stolen property. Appellee appealed to the Superior Court, contending that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his second-degree murder conviction because he had been acquitted of the predicate offense of robbery. The Superior Court reversed appellee's second-degree murder conviction and vacated his judgment of sentence upon concluding that since the jury had acquitted him of robbery, the Commonwealth failed to prove that he killed the victim while in perpetration of a robbery. The court held that the Superior Court erred by concluding that a jury's verdict of guilt on a second-degree murder charge and its acquittal on the predicate felony of robbery were impermissibly inconsistent and could not stand. The court found that the Superior Court misapplied case law precedent and should have accepted the inconsistent verdicts.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the Superior Court and remanded the case to the trial court for reinstatement of appellee's conviction for second-degree murder.

Commonwealth v. Porter


No. 557 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


613 Pa. 510; 35 A.3d 4; 2012 Pa. LEXIS 135


March 8, 2010, Submitted 
January 19, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order entered on November 8, 2007 in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Philadelphia County dismissing the PCRA petition at No. CP-51-CR-0622491-1985. Trial Court Judge: Peter F. Rogers, Judge.
Commonwealth v. Porter, 35 A.3d 4, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 133 (Pa., Jan. 19, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a death-sentenced prisoner, challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which denied his serial petition under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9541-9546, as untimely. At issue in the case was a pending federal Atkins petition along with appellant's serial Brady PCRA petition.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's PCRA petition raised a Brady claim. The PCRA court determined that appellant's Brady claim was time barred. The court agreed, finding that it was not proper for the PCRA court to separate the issues of appellant's Brady and Atkins claims. The court criticized the practice of holding a serial PCRA petition in abeyance pending federal review of appellant's previously exhausted claims. As a result, the court held that the order below was appealable and, on the merits. The court addressed the circumstances creating uncertainty respecting appealability, in an effort to ensure that those circumstances do not arise again; and the circumstances creating the unacceptable delay in the case at bar, so that PCRA courts throughout Pennsylvania will take measures to avoid such delays. The court ultimately directed the PCRA court to promptly dispose of appellant's long-pending prior PCRA petition, which raised an Atkins issue. The court held there was no reason to hold a serial PCRA petition merely because federal cross-appeals were pending on already-exhausted habeas claims.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the PCRA court. The court directed the PCRA court to decide the pending Atkins petition as expeditiously as possible.

Commonwealth v. Sanchez

SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


614 Pa. 1; 36 A.3d 24; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 3095


September 15, 2010, Argued

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Entered on March 30, 2009, in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Lancaster County at CP-36-CR-0003550-2007. Trial Court Judge: Joseph C. Madenspacher, President Judge.
Commonwealth v. Kebede, 23 A.3d 1080, 2011 Pa. Super. LEXIS 320 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction and the judgment of sentence of the Lancaster County Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), following a jury trial finding him guilty of first degree murder, in violation of 18 Pa.C.S. § 2501(a), and other crimes, and which imposed the sentence of death against him. The court conducted its mandatory review under 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711(d)(6).


OVERVIEW: The court first addressed the sufficiency of the evidence and found that the record established sufficient evidence to support appellant's first-degree murder conviction based on the uncontested forensic evidence and eyewitness testimony that the victim had been shot three times with a small caliber weapon, and that the killing was with malice and intentional because the deadly weapon was used on vital parts of the victim's body. The court found that the conviction was supported by testimony from co-defendants and eyewitnesses as to the identity of the shooter, including testimony that appellant boasted about shooting the victim. The court took the opportunity to devise a procedure for implementing the Atkins decision in Pennsylvania and approved of the procedure of a trial court submitting a colorable Atkins mental retardation issue to the jury for a penalty phase decision. The court placed the burden on the proponent of the Atkins claim, usually the defendant, to prove mental retardation by a preponderance of the evidence and held that such a finding must be unanimous.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and judgment of sentence.

Estate of Fridenberg v. Commonwealth


No. 32 EAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


613 Pa. 281; 33 A.3d 581; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 2820


March 8, 2011, Argued 
November 23, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Judgment of Superior Court entered on 8/24/2009 at No. 2119 EDA 2008 (reargument denied 10/30/2009), reversing the Order entered on 7/1/2008 and remanding to the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Orphans' Court at No. 261 of 1941.
Estate of Fridenberg, 2009 PA Super 164, 982 A.2d 68, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2814 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Office of Attorney General (OAG), sought review of an order from the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed a trial court order that held that appellee trustee bank (TB) was not entitled to receive commissions from principal pursuant to 20 Pa.C.S. § 7185 for its trust administration services.


OVERVIEW: A decedent executed a last will and testament, which provided, inter alia, for a charitable remainder. An individual and the TB were appointed as estate executors and trustees under the will. The TB filed a third account of the trust after the co-trustee died, which requested commissions out of the principal for both trustees. The trial court upheld the OAG's objection, finding that when the trust was created in 1940, more than one commission from principal was barred. The superior court reversed based upon the numerous legislative enactments that permitted more than one commission for previously established trusts. On further review, the court found that stare decisis did not apply, as the responsibilities of trustees had changed drastically since the creation of the trust. Allowing retroactive application of § 7185 did not violate the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, as retroactive application was a rational means of the legislature to accomplish its purpose of allowing proper and fair compensation to trustees administering trusts after February 18, 1982. Further, there was no violation of the beneficiaries' Contracts Clause rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the superior court.

In re Estate of Sauers


No. 78 MAP 2009


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


613 Pa. 186; 32 A.3d 1241; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 2832; 53 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 1044


May 11, 2010, Argued 
November 23, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal of the Order of the Superior Court entered April 17, 2009 at No. 1060 MDA 2007 (Application for Reargument filed on May 21, 2008 was Granted and Opinion of May 9, 2008 was Withdrawn) Affirming the Order of the York County Court of Common Pleas entered May 16, 2007 at No. 67-06-01327. Appeal Allowed September 11, 2009 at 331 MAL 2009. Trial Court Judge: Gregory M. Snyder, Judge. Intermediate Ct. Judges: Correale F. Stevens, John L. Musmanno, Richard B. Klein, John T. Bender, Mary Jane Bowes, Suan Peikes Gantman, Jacqueline O. Shogan, Robert A. Freedberg, and John M. Cleland, JJ..
In re Estate of Sauers, 2009 PA Super 75, 971 A.2d 1265, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 89 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former wife (FW) of a decedent, sought review of an order from the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a trial court order. The trial court had determined that appellee estate administrator (EA) could distribute proceeds from the decedent's life insurance policy (LIP) to a contingent beneficiary (CB) pursuant to 20 Pa.C.S. § 6111.2, rather than to the FW as the primary beneficiary (PB).


OVERVIEW: The decedent obtained a life insurance policy pursuant to an employee group benefit plan, and he named his FW as the PB and a nephew as the CB. Although the decedent and the FW divorced, the beneficiaries remained unchanged at the time of the decedent's death. The trial court determined that the EA had the capacity to bring a show cause petition regarding the proceeds of the LIP, and that the court had subject matter jurisdiction. It found that under 20 Pa.C.S. § 6111.2 and 29 U.S.C.S. § 1144(a) of the Employee Retirement Income Security Act, 29 U.S.C.S. § 1001 et seq., the CB was entitled to the proceeds. That decision was affirmed. On further review, the court found that under 20 Pa.C.S. §§ 711(1), 6108, and 3311(a), the EA had the authority and capacity to file the action in the orphans' court. However, it found that the disbursement of LIP proceeds constituted the ongoing administration of an employee benefit plan, and not merely a onetime payment of benefits, pursuant to § 6111.2 and 29 U.S.C.S. § 1002(1). The court concluded that § 6111.2 was superseded and preempted by the vast reach of ERISA under 29 U.S.C.S. §§ 1104(a)(1)(D) and 1144(a).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the Superior Court, and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

 

124 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Commonwealth v. May


No. 600 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


612 Pa. 505; 31 A.3d 668; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 2833


September 15, 2010, Argued 
November 23, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by May v. Pa., 2012 U.S. LEXIS 6061 (U.S., Oct. 1, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered on October 15, 2008 in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Lebanon County at No. CP-38-CR-0000071-1990 (The trial court reinstated right to appeal NPT on September 30, 2009). Trial Court Judge: John C. Tylwalk, President Judge, Robert J. Eby, Senior Judge.
Commonwealth v. May, 551 Pa. 286, 710 A.2d 44, 1998 Pa. LEXIS 584 (1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following his third penalty phase, defendant was sentenced to death in the Court of Common Pleas of Lebanon County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), after he was convicted of first degree murder, in violation of 18 Pa.C.S. § 2502(a). He appealed his sentence.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held it was not error to let the prosecution argue the "significant history" aggravator in 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711(d)(9) because (1) it was not unconstitutionally vague, and (2) the fact that convictions underlying it arose from an incident occurring after the murder defendant was sentenced for did not reduce their relevance. Inconsistent verdicts as to the circumstance did not show vagueness because (1) various factors contributed to each verdict, and (2) no correlation between aggravators and mitigators and vagueness was shown. Basing the aggravator on post-murder convictions did not offend ex post facto or due process protections because "significant history" referred to his status at sentencing. He was not entitled to 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711(h)(3) relief because (1) nothing raised was objected to below, and (2) it had to be considered on collateral review in an ineffective assistance claim. The sentence was proper because (1) it was not the result of passion, prejudice, or another arbitrary factor and was based on sufficient evidence of murder, and (2) sufficient evidence showed a significant history of felony convictions involving use or threat of violence to the person.


OUTCOME: The sentence was affirmed.


 

125 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Sinnott


No. 11 EAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


612 Pa. 321; 30 A.3d 1105; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 2675


March 8, 2011, Argued 
November 2, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Superior Court entered on 6/23/2009 at No. 158 EDA 2008 affirming in part and reversing in part the Judgment of Sentence entered on November 26, 2007 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Criminal Division at No. CP-51-CR-00078913-2007. Trial Court Judge: Thomas E. Dempsey, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: John T. Bender, Jacqueline O. Shogan, JJ., Stephen J. McEwen, Jr., President Judge Emeritus.
Commonwealth v. Sinnott, 2009 PA Super 114, 976 A.2d 1184, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1368 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a bench trial, defendant was convicted of simple assault, terroristic threats, ethnic intimidation, and possession of instruments of crime. The Superior Court (Pennsylvania) reversed the conviction for ethnic intimidation, 18 Pa.C.S. § 2710, on the basis of insufficient evidence. The Commonwealth appealed.


OVERVIEW: The charges arose out of defendant's conduct and comments made to the daughter of his employer. The intermediate appellate court held that defendant's ethnically derogatory remarks and behavior were the product of circumstances unrelated to the victim's race, specifically, defendant's anger with the victim's father over an employment issue. However, the appellate court found that § 2710's intent element was satisfied by evidence that ethnic malice was a motivator for defendant's criminal act; it did not need to be the sole motivator. Evidence existed that defendant was motivated by racial animus; he made repeated references to the victim's national origin while behaving in a menacing manner. Evidence also existed that defendant was motivated by his anger with the victim's father; however, the victim was not the father but the daughter. The trial court, found sufficient evidence existed to conclude that defendant's conduct was motivated by malicious intent toward the victim's ethnicity. The intermediate appellate court failed to view the evidence of defendant's repeated derogatory ethnic slurs in the light most favorable to the Commonwealth.


OUTCOME: The portion of the intermediate appellate court's order reversing the ethnic intimidation conviction was reversed, and the judgment of sentence was reinstated for that offense. In all other aspects, the order was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Chamberlain

SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

October 14, 2011, Decided

US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Chamberlain v. Pennsylvania, 132 S. Ct. 2377, 182 L. Ed. 2d 1017, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 3446 (U.S., 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the order entered on July 10, 2006 in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Bradford County at No. CP-08-CR-0000226-1993. Trial Court Judge Jeffrey A. Smith, President Judge.
Commonwealth v. Chamberlain, 557 Pa. 34, 731 A.2d 593, 1999 Pa. LEXIS 1689 (1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of sentence of the Bradford County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of two counts of first-degree murder, as well as burglary and possession of an instrument of crime, in violation of 18 Pa.C.S. §§ 2502(a), 3502, and 907. OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted based on the shooting deaths of his estranged wife and her boyfriend. A prior appeal resulted in a remand in order to allow defendant to obtain DNA testing of certain blood evidence. P. 230(B)(2) with respect to grand jury testimony. 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711 was not unconstitutional, and imposition of the death sentence was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence of the trial court.

 

127 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Hart


No. 9 MAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


611 Pa. 531; 28 A.3d 898; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 2334


March 8, 2011, Argued 
September 28, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on February 5, 2009 at No. 285 EDA 2008 affirming the Judgment of Sentence entered on January 3, 2008 in the Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County, Criminal Division, at No. 3057-2006. Trial Court Judge: Ann A. Osborne, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: John T. Bender, Judge, Jack A. Panella, Judge, John T.J. Kelly, Jr., Judge.
Commonwealth v. Hart, 970 A.2d 468, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 953 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a bench trial, defendant was convicted of four counts of attempted luring of a child into a motor vehicle and sentenced to 18 months' probation. A three judge panel of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) affirmed defendant's conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the supreme court was whether a person who offered a ride without previously obtaining the permission of a parent of the child, but who otherwise lacked criminal intent to harm the child, could be convicted of luring a child into a motor vehicle under 18 Pa.C.S. § 2910. The supreme court held that an attempt to lure under 18 Pa.C.S. § 2910 does not occur upon the mere offer of a ride in a motor vehicle to a child, but, rather, involves only situations where a child is provided a further enticement or inducement to enter the vehicle, in addition to the offer of the ride, particularly under such circumstances which suggest the child is being led into a potentially harmful situation. That enticement or inducement might be the promise of a pleasurable reward for entry into the vehicle such as receiving money or a treat. Likewise, a similar attractive temptation could be created with the promise of the opportunity for the child to view an object of interest like a toy, a game, or a puppy. The enticement or inducement need not necessarily be express, but could arise where the enticement and inducement is evident from the circumstances accompanying the making of an offer.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the superior court was reversed and defendant was discharged.

 

128 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Piper Group, Inc. v. Bedminster Twp. Bd. of Supervisors


No. 75 MAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


612 Pa. 282; 30 A.3d 1083; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 2333


May 11, 2011, Argued 
September 28, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 1824 CD 2009 dated March 12, 2010, Reconsideration Denied May 10, 2010, Affirming the Order of the Bucks County Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, at No. 07-03457-29-5 dated August 27, 2009. Trial Court Judge: Theodore C. Fritsch, Jr., Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Dan Pellegrini, Judge, Johnny J. Butler, Judge, Rochelle S. Friedman, Senior Judge.
Piper Group, Inc. v. Bedminster Twp. Bd. of Supervisors, 992 A.2d 224, 2010 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 128 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sued appellees, a township and its board of supervisors, challenging the constitutionality of a zoning ordinance. The trial court rejected appellant's "cure challenge" to the ordinance's constitutionality, finding that a new ordinance enacted by the board cured the constitutional defects of the prior ordinance. Appellant sought review; the Pennsylvania Commonwealth Court affirmed. Appellant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The high court had invalidated an ordinance that imposed a one acre minimum lot size for residences in an agriculture zone, finding it unconstitutionally restricted landowners' development rights. Appellant then filed a "cure challenge" with the board under §§ 609.1 and 916.1(a)(2) (53 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 10609.1 and 10916.1(a)(2)) of the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code (MPC), consisting of a proposed new ordinance and a development plan. The board, pursuant to § 609.2(1) (53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10609.2(1)) of the MPC, then declared the old ordinance invalid and enacted a new ordinance, which reduced the minimum lot size. After hearings, it rejected appellant's cure challenge. The high court held the lower courts erred by holding the board had a moratorium period to consider the high court's decision before acting on the cure challenge, as long as it "acted quickly." But as the new ordinance severed the unconstitutional aspects of the old and cured the de facto defects in the district that included appellant's land, and as the board followed the procedure of § 609.1, appellant had not been automatically entitled to develop its land at the specific density that it proposed.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Travaglia


No. 571 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


611 Pa. 481; 28 A.3d 868; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 2335


September 15, 2009, Argued 
September 28, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Travaglia v. Pa., 132 S. Ct. 1908, 182 L. Ed. 2d 777, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 2874 (U.S., Apr. 16, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Criminal Division, entered on July 26, 2005 at No. CP-65-CR-0000684-1980. Trial Court Judge: John E. Blahovec, President Judge.
Commonwealth v. Travaglia, 2002 PA Super 37, 792 A.2d 1261, 2002 Pa. Super. LEXIS 156 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Westmoreland County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) of first-degree murder of a police officer and conspiracy and sentenced to death following a penalty hearing held pursuant to a grant of federal habeas corpus relief. Defendant appealed his sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said it was error to bar testimony on his future ability to adjust to a prison's general population. The supreme court held an erroneous finding that the evidence was irrelevant as a mitigating factor was harmless because Pa. R. Evid. 602 barred the witness's personal opinion. Testimony purportedly showing defendant's religious conversion was properly barred because (1) it did not make the claim more or less likely, and (2) the claim was otherwise admitted. It was no error to admit, to rebut defendant's claim of a positive prison adjustment, evidence of his pre-incarceration crimes because 42 Pa.C.S. § 5918(1) allowed such evidence. After the federal court barred his guilty plea to another murder, it was no error to deny a mistrial when the prosecutor mentioned the plea because (1) the federal court only barred the plea's use to prove an aggravating circumstance, under 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711(d)(9), and (2) it was used to test his character witnesses' awareness of his past crimes. It was no error to admit the other murder's circumstances because (1) they showed motive, intent, or absence of mistake, and (2) the jury was told not to consider them as an aggravating circumstance.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Foster


No. 7 EAP 2009


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


609 Pa. 502; 17 A.3d 332; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 681


October 20, 2009, Argued 
March 29, 2011, Decided 
January 18, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
RE-SUBMITTED: January 18, 2011.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court entered on October 22, 2008 at No. 3450 EDA 2006 Vacating and Remanding the Judgment of Sentence entered on November 30, 2006 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. CP-51-CR-0109521-2006.
Commonwealth v. Foster, 2008 PA Super 252, 960 A.2d 160, 2008 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3504 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a bench trial, defendant was convicted of robbery, conspiracy, theft, and possession of an instrument of crime. The trial court imposed a mandatory minimum sentence pursuant to § 9712(a) (42 Pa.C.S. § 9712(a)) of the Pennsylvania Judicial Code and denied defendant's subsequent motion for modification of sentence. Defendant appealed; the Pennsylvania Superior Court reversed his sentence. The Commonwealth appealed.


OVERVIEW: Though defendant had not visibly possessed a firearm during the commission of the robbery, the trial court imposed the mandatory minimum sentence under § 9712(a) because his co-conspirator had done so. Defendant's subsequent post-sentence motions and direct appeal did not concern sentencing issues. After the high court decided Commonwealth v. Dickson, which held that § 9712(a) did not apply to any "unarmed co-conspirators," defendant challenged his sentence under Dickson. The Commonwealth argued as his challenge involved solely discretionary aspects of his sentence, he waived it by failing to raise it in his post-sentence motions or in a Pa.R.A.P. 2119(f) statement of reasons relied on for allowance of appeal. The high court disagreed. As a "Dickson challenge" by an unarmed co-conspirator implicated the legality of the unarmed coconspirator's sentence for purposes of issue preservation and waiver, Dickson challenges could not be waived based on a defendant's failure to raise them in post-sentence motions, or, likewise, for a defendant's failure to file a statement of reasons relied upon for allowance of appeal pursuant to Rule 2119(f).


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant objectors challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which awarded costs of litigation to appellee candidate, pursuant to the Election Code, 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2937. The objectors were unsuccessfully in seeking to have the candidate's nomination petition set aside with regard to a senatorial seat.

OUTCOME: The court reversed the order.

In re Nomination Petition of Farnese


No. 6 EAP 2008


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


609 Pa. 573; 17 A.3d 375; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 682



March 29, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Opinion and Order of the Commonwealth Court (Friedman, J.) dated March 10, 2008 at No. 121 M.D. 2008 denying the Petition to Set Aside the Nomination Petition of Lawrence M. Farnese, Jr.
In re Farnese, 596 Pa. 464, 944 A.2d 752, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 398 (2008)
In re Nomination Petition of Farnese, 605 Pa. 375, 989 A.2d 1274, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 2477 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant objectors brought a challenge to the nomination petition of appellee candidate pursuant to 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2869. The Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) denied the petition of the objectors. The objectors appealed.


OVERVIEW: The candidate agreed that over 60 percent of the signatures contained in his nomination petition were invalid. The appellate court disagreed with the trial court's assessment that allegations of a pattern of fraud were immaterial in a case involving objections to a nomination petition. However, although the objectors' brief included numerous allegations that the tactics of the circulators to procure the allegedly illegitimate signatures were known to the candidate and the campaign manager, the objectors did not make this specific objection in their petition to set aside the nomination petition. Although the candidate himself was the circulator of six separate signature pages, none of those pages was withdrawn, and none of the signatures on those pages was stipulated to as invalid. The case law of Pennsylvania that could have been read to support the proposition that a false affidavit contained in a nomination petition may have been egregious enough to void the petition altogether and remove the candidate from the ballot, applied to intentional and knowing falsehoods affirmed by the candidate personally that were designed to deceive the electorate.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the PCRA court granting defendant a new trial and a new penalty hearing and dismissed the petition for PCRA relief.

Brayman Constr. Corp. v. DOT


Nos. 12 & 45 MAP 2009


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


608 Pa. 584; 13 A.3d 925; 2011 Pa. LEXIS 383


May 12, 2010, Argued 
February 22, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Summary judgment granted by, Injunction granted at Brayman Constr. Corp. v. Commonwealth DOT, 30 A.3d 560, 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 511 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of Commonwealth Court at No. 527 MD 2008 dated February 17, 2009. Trial Court Judge: Dan Pellegrini, Judge.
Brayman Constr. Corp. v. DOT, 2009 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 869 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contractor challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which preliminary enjoined appellee, the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation (PennDOT), from using a particular method of awarding bids on public contracts but denied relief as to a bridge project. PennDOT cross-appealed. The contractor filed a petition for review in the nature of a complaint in equity directed to the court's original jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: PennDOT decided to rebuild two bridges and, for the first time, used innovative bidding methods for selecting contractors for highway projects outlined in a publication. Pursuant to that publication, PennDOT issued an advertisement seeking statements of interest and then short-listed three firms. The contractor issued a timely statement of interest but was not short-listed. The contractor's petition for review challenged the innovative process, contending it was contrary to the Procurement Code, 62 Pa.C.S. §§ 101-4509, and compromised the competitive bidding process. The Commonwealth Code agreed that the innovative procurement method, as implemented by PennDOT, was overly subjective and not authorized by the code and enjoined PennDOT from seeking and evaluating contracts under that method but did not apply its injunction to the bridge project due to the safety issues. The court agreed that the contractor was aggrieved and entitled to challenge PennDOT since it was eliminated from contention during the short-listing step. Thus, the Commonwealth Court had equity jurisdiction in light of the exclusive remedy set forth in 62 Pa.C.S. § 1711.1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and remanded the case to the Commonwealth Court for further proceedings.

Commonwealth v. Briggs, 2005 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 503, 76 Pa. D. & C.4th 225 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the sentence of death imposed by the Bradford County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) after a jury found him guilty of two counts of first-degree murder, in violation of 18 Pa.C.S. § 2502, and one count of robbery, in violation of 18 Pa.C.S. § 3701. OVERVIEW: Appellant's convictions arose from the shooting death of two sheriff deputies, who had gone to the property of appellant's father to arrest appellant on outstanding warrants. The court overall found no reversible error.

Johnson v. Am. Std.


No. 43 EAP 2009, No. 44 EAP 2009, No. 45 EAP 2009


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


607 Pa. 492; 8 A.3d 318; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2592; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P18,526


March 9, 2010, Argued 
November 18, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court entered on February 6, 2009 at No. 2954 EDA 2006 Affirming the Order entered on October 3, 2006 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Civil Division, at No. 3609 November Term, 2005. Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court entered on February 6, 2009 at No. 2955 EDA 2006 Affirming the Order entered on October 3, 2006 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Civil Division, at No. 2154 November Term 2004. Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court entered on February 6, 2009 at No. 2956 EDA 2006 Affirming the Order entered on October 3, 2006 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Civil Division, at No. 1631 November Term, 2004.
Johnson v. Am. Std., 2009 PA Super 22, 966 A.2d 573, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 29 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff estates brought suits against defendant manufacturer for asbestos exposure. In response to defendant's global summary judgment motion, they argued that 15 Pa.C.S. § 1929.1 was unconstitutional under the dormant Commerce Clause and the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The trial courts granted summary judgment for defendant, and the Pennsylvania Superior Court affirmed. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court reiterated that in Pennsylvania, a party had to be aggrieved in order to possess standing to pursue litigation. Aggrievability was obtained by having a substantial, direct, and immediate interest in proceedings or litigation. When the standards for substantiality, directness, and immediacy were readily met, the inquiry into aggrievability, and therefore standing, ended. Should, however, a party's immediate interest not be apparent, a zone of interests analysis could be employed. Here, plaintiffs had standing to pursue their constitutional challenges. First, their interests were substantial, as their lawsuits were subject to dismissal under § 1929.1. Second, there was a direct connection between the constitutional violations asserted and the harm complained of, as defendant was invoking the protections of a potentially unconstitutional statute to obtain automatic and irreversible dismissal of plaintiffs' causes of action. Finally, plaintiffs' interests were immediate, because only defendant's invocation of § 1929.1 caused the dismissal of plaintiffs' claims. The ability of plaintiffs to recover from other manufacturers was of no concern within the instant analysis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the Superior Court, which had affirmed the trial courts' grants of summary judgment. It remanded the appeal to the Superior Court for full consideration of the merits of plaintiffs' constitutional claims.

Cash Am. Net of Nev., LLC v. Dep't of Banking


No. 68 MAP 2009


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


607 Pa. 432; 8 A.3d 282; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2386


May 11, 2010, Argued 
October 19, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 8 MD 2009, entered on July 10, 2009. Trial Court Judge: Bonnie Brigance Leadbetter, President Judge; Doris A. Smith-Ribner, Johnny J. Butler, Mary Hannah Leavitt, Bernard L. McGinley, Renee Cohn Jubelirer, Robert E. Simpson, JJ.
Cash Am. Net of Nev., LLC v. Dep't of Banking, 978 A.2d 1028, 2009 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 570 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant limited liability company filed a petition for review in the nature of a complaint in equity against appellees, the Department of Banking and the Secretary of Banking. The Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) granted summary judgment to appellees. It held that the Department had the authority to apply 7 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 6203.A to lenders without offices or personnel in Pennsylvania. Appellant challenged the trial court's decision.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was in the business of making short-term "payday" loans to Pennsylvania residents over the Internet. It was not licensed under the Consumer Discount Company Act (CDCA), 7 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 6201-6219. The court stated that the plain language of § 6203.A made it unlawful for any unlicensed lender to make the specified types of loans in the Commonwealth, regardless of whether it was physically located or had personnel in the Commonwealth. Next, the court rejected appellant's argument that the Department's new interpretation of § 6203.A as explained in a notice was the promulgation of a new regulation, as opposed to a statement of policy. In issuing the notice, the Department did not invoke its authority to issue binding rules and regulations. It very clearly expressed that the notice articulated its interpretation of the law. Next, because appellant sought a declaration that it was not violating the CDCA, it could not complain that the trial court lacked authority to declare it was violating the CDCA. Finally, appellant had waived its argument that it would be subject to a retroactive application of the trial court's order. Moreover, the argument appeared to be hypothetical.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order.

In re Nomination Petitions & Papers of Stevenson


No. 54 MAP 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


608 Pa. 397; 12 A.3d 273; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2274



October 4, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at In re Stevenson, 40 A.3d 1212, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 631 (Pa., 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered on August 19, 2010 at No. 643 M.D. 2010.
In Re: The Nomination Petitions and Papers of Carl Stevenson As A Candidate For State Representative In The 134th Legislative Dist., 2010 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 918 (2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee objectors brought a petition to set aside appellant candidate's nominating paper. The Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) granted the petition and ordered the Secretary of the Commonwealth to strike the candidate's name from the general election ballot for the office of state representative. The candidate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The objectors asserted a challenge to individual signatures the candidate's nominating paper as well as a "global" challenge to three pages of signatures on the paper because the circulator of those pages was not a resident of the district, in violation of 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2911(d). The candidate argued that the global challenge failed because § 2911(d) was unconstitutional under the First Amendment to the extent it imposed a residency requirement. The trial court rejected the First Amendment claim, but failed to decide a potentially dispositive non-constitutional challenge. The decision on the global challenge was not sustainable on the record. Neither the lower court nor the objectors forwarded any substantive justification of the rejection of this argument. Nor did the lower court or the objectors respond to the candidate's related claim of reliance on the Secretary's actions and directions in altering its nominating paper form. The power to not be bound by lower federal court rulings did not absolve a state court of its obligation to decide properly presented federal claims on the merits. Nothing in the record or the pleadings provided a basis to affirm the decision.


OUTCOME: The judgment was vacated and the case was remanded for an immediate hearing to determine the individual signature challenges asserted in the petition. The trial court was directed to issue an order forthwith either dismissing or sustaining the challenge, and implementing its decision as necessary. Jurisdiction was otherwise retained.

In re Opening a Private Rd. ex rel. O'Reilly


No. 10 WAP 2009


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


607 Pa. 280; 5 A.3d 246; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2272


September 16, 2009, Argued; August 16, 2010, Resubmitted 
September 30, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Remanded by O'Reilly v. Hickory on the Green Homeowners Ass'n, 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 224 (Pa. Commw. Ct., May 5, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered July 11, 2008 at No. 2214 CD 2007 affirming the Order of the Allegheny County Court of Common Pleas entered November 9, 2007 at No. GD04-02972.
In re Opening a Private Rd., 954 A.2d 57, 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 319 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed a trial court order that the Private Road Act (PRA), 36 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 2731-2891, did not effect an unconstitutional taking of private property and overruled appellants' objections to appellee landlocked landowner's suit seeking to open a private road. Appellee brought suit under the PRA seeking a connection to a public road.


OVERVIEW: Appellee asserted that his land became landlocked as a result of the Commonwealth's exercise of eminent domain to build an interstate. He commenced the proceeding under the PRA to open a private road to connect his property to a public road within a residential development wherein appellants resided. The Commonwealth Court, in affirming the trial court, determined that all lands in Pennsylvania were encumbered with a six percent incorporeal burden for the building of a public road system, which included private roads and that the creation of a private road under the PRA did not constitute a taking in the ordinary sense. The Court found no support for the Commonwealth Court's conclusion and held that a physical invasion and permanent occupation of private property, such as that which would be accomplished by the creation of a private road under the PRA was a taking. The Court found that the Commonwealth Court had neither supplied a sufficient rationale to support its theory of a statewide incorporeal burden nor put into application the prevailing standard governing takings.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order and remanded the case to the Commonwealth Court for further review.

W. Mifflin Area Sch. Dist. v. Zahorchak


No. 48 WAP 2008


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


607 Pa. 153; 4 A.3d 1042; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2174


April 13, 2010, Argued 
September 29, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered September 4, 2008 at No. 371 MD 2007.
W. Mifflin Area Sch. Dist. v. Zahorchak, 956 A.2d 1040, 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 391 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school districts sued appellees, the Pennsylvania Department of Education, the Secretary of Education, a school district, and an education association, and seeking injunctive relief and a declaratory judgment stating that 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 11-1113(b.2) and 1607.1 were special laws in violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 32. The Pennsylvania Commonwealth Court denied relief and dismissed the petition. Appellants sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Appellee district was a financially distressed school district that had closed its only high school. Appellants were potential recipients of its high school students under § 1607.1(a). The high court noted that appellee district was the only school district in Pennsylvania that met all of the criteria of § 1607.1(a), and that a highly improbable convergence of events would be necessary for any other school district to be affected by §§ 11-1113(b.2) and 1607.1 As the class created by § 1607.1 was, at a minimum, "substantially closed" to new members, in violation of the dictates of Harrisburg School District v. Hickok and Pennsylvania Turnpike Commission v. Commonwealth, §§ 11-1113(b.2) and 1607.1 amounted to special legislation prohibited by Pa. Const. art. III, § 32.


OUTCOME: The order of the intermediate appellate court was reversed and the matter was remanded to that tribunal for further proceedings.

Freed v. Geisinger Med. Ctr.


No. 77 MAP 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


607 Pa. 225; 5 A.3d 212; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2167


December 2, 2009, Argued 
September 29, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the order of the Superior Court dated September 29, 2006, at No. 819 MDA 2005 reversing the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County at No. 200-2613. 2006 PA Super 274, 910 A.2d 68 (Pa. Super. 2006). Appeal allowed August 2, 2007 at 3 MAL 2007. Trial Court Judge: Charles C. Brown, Jr., Senior Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Correale F. Stevens, Seamus P. McCaffery, Jack A. Panella, JJ.
Freed v. Geisinger Med. Ctr., 601 Pa. 233, 971 A.2d 1202, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1124 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee sued appellant hospital for malpractice. The trial court granted the hospital summary judgment; the Pennsylvania Superior Court reversed. The high court affirmed, holding that an otherwise competent and qualified nurse was not prohibited by the Pennsylvania Professional Nursing Law (PNL), 63 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 211 et seq., from giving expert testimony at trial regarding medical causation. It granted the hospital's motion for reargument.


OVERVIEW: In Flanagan v. Labe, the high court had held that a nurse could not offer opinion testimony as to the identity and cause of a medical condition since such testimony constituted a medical diagnosis, which a nurse was precluded from making under the PNL. In affirming the intermediate appellate court, the high court sua sponte reversed Flanagan, and ruled that its decision applied retroactively to the parties in the instant case. The hospital argued that the sua sponte reversal and its retroactive application denied it its due process rights of notice and an opportunity to be heard. The high court held that while neither party had argued that Flanagan should be overruled, it was appropriate for the high court to examine the viability of Flanagan in rendering its decision. In its prior opinion, the high court concluded that the rule established in Flanagan was akin to a procedural ruling, and thus its reversal of Flanagan properly was applied retroactively. The hospital offered no argument or case law to suggest this determination was in error. It offered no argument in its brief that the high court had not already considered in reaching the decision set forth in its original opinion.


OUTCOME: The high court reaffirmed its prior decision.
