Bd. of Revision of Taxes v. City of Philadelphia


No. 98 EM 2010


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


607 Pa. 104; 4 A.3d 610; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2134


September 20, 2010, Submitted 
September 20, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Judgment entered by, in part, Objection sustained by, in part, Objection overruled by, in part Bd. of Revision v. City of Phila., 2011 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 151 (June 17, 2011)
Related proceeding at Nigro v. City of Phila., 2017 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 143 (Apr. 10, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Application for Leave to File Original Process. Application for the Exercise of Original Jurisdiction and King's Bench Power.
Bd. of Revision of Taxes v. City of Philadelphia, 605 Pa. 667, 993 A.2d 873, 2010 Pa. LEXIS 883 (Apr. 27, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent city enacted a reorganization ordinance (RO) abolishing the Philadelphia Board of Revision of Taxes (BRT). Petitioners, the BRT and its members, individually and in their official capacities, filed applications for leave to file original process and for the exercise of original jurisdiction, seeking to halt implementation of the RO and a salary ordinance (SO) by a preliminary injunction or by quo warranto, prohibition, and mandamus.


OVERVIEW: The RO, ratified by the electorate, abolished the BRT and replaced it with two new bodies. As the RO's validity was of immediate public concern, the issue was one of law, and petitioners had a clear right to relief, the high court exercised plenary jurisdiction over the issue under 42 Pa.C.S. § 726. It struck the BRT and its members, to the extent they sued in their official capacities, as parties, leaving only members suing as individuals, as the stricken parties did not have the city solicitor's authorization to prosecute the case with private counsel. The RO's transfer of the BRT's adjudicative function to a board of appeals was not authorized 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 13132(c) and was ultra vires; petitioners were entitled to a permanent injunction preventing implementation of that portion of the RO. The other provisions of the RO were valid and severable. The issue of the validity of the SO, which reduced BRT members' salaries, did not require the high court's accelerated review. Quo warranto was not warranted, as there was no challenge to anyone's right to public office, and the permanent injunction rendered the request for a preliminary injunction, mandamus, and prohibition moot.


OUTCOME: The high court entered a permanent injunction barring enforcement of the RO to the extent it abolished the BRT with respect to its appellate function; transferred BRT employees to the board of appeals; and provided that the board of appeals would perform the BRT's appellate function. Petitioners' requests for a preliminary injunction, quo warranto, prohibition, and mandamus were dismissed. The high court did not review their challenge to the SO.

In the Interest of F.C. III


No. 17 WAP 2009


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


607 Pa. 45; 2 A.3d 1201; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 1799


September 15, 2009, Argued 
August 17, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered January 23, 2009 at No. 1302 WDA 2007, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County dated June 12, 2007 at No. 1081-07. Appeal allowed July 13, 2009 at 199 WAL 2009. Trial Court Judge: Guido A. DeAngelis, Judge. Internediate Court Judges: John L. Musmanno, Christine Donohue, Robert E. Colville, JJ.
In the Interest of F.C., III, 2009 PA Super 9, 966 A.2d 1131, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 16 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) which upheld the constitutionality of 71 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1690.112a (Act 53) of the Pennsylvania Drug and Alcohol Abuse Control Act. The Superior Court also denied the juvenile's contention that he was denied due process by virtue of his restraint prior to and during his hearing. The juvenile argued that Act 53 violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The juvenile's grandmother filed a petition pursuant to Act 53 to compel him to receive drug and alcohol abuse treatment on an involuntary basis. The Juvenile Court, entered a preliminary order appointing counsel for the juvenile and ordered him to undergo a drug and alcohol assessment by a licensed drug addiction counselor. It also directed him to appear for a hearing. Sheriffs deputies took custody of him at his home and transported him to the hearing. The juvenile court granted the grandmother's petition and ordered the juvenile to receive inpatient treatment for marijuana dependency. The Pennsylvania Supreme Court concluded that Act 53 provided sufficient protection to pass constitutional muster. An assessment rendered by the statutorily-prescribed professionals satisfied the requirement of a decision made by appropriate medical personnel. The protections provided by Act 53 at the hearing to determine the propriety of treatment met the minimum protections mandated by U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The juvenile court did not abuse its discretion in requiring the juvenile to remain restrained during the brief Act 53 proceedings.


OUTCOME: The constitutional challenge was rejected. The order of the lower court was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Martin


No. 441 CAP, No. 442 CAP, No. 443 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


607 Pa. 165; 5 A.3d 177; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2866


June 2, 2005, Submitted 
August 17, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Martin v. Pennsylvania, 131 S. Ct. 2960, 180 L. Ed. 2d 250, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 4206 (U.S., 2011)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Sentence imposed by Commonwealth v. Martin, 2014 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 216 (2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
441 CAP

Appeal from the Order entered on March 4, 2004 denying Portions of PCRA petition in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Lebanon County at Nos. 1993-10899 & 1993-11079. Trial Court Judge: Robert J. Eby, Senior Judge.

442 CAP

Appeal from the Order entered on March 4, 2004 granting new sentencing hearing in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Lebanon County at Nos. 1993-10899 & 1993-11079. Trial Court Judge: Robert J. Eby, Senior Judge.

443 CAP

Appeal from the Order entered on March 4, 2004 denying Portions of PCRA petition in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Lebanon County at Nos. 1993-10899 & 1993-11079. Trial Court Judge: Robert J. Eby, Senior Judge.
Commonwealth v. Martin, 561 Pa. 145, 748 A.2d 1233, 2000 Pa. LEXIS 731 (2000)
Commonwealth v. Martin, 1 A.3d 868, 2010 Pa. LEXIS 1806 (Pa., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a capital case, the Court of Common Pleas of Lebanon County (Pennsylvania) denied appellant inmate's guilt phase claims under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9541-9546, but granted a new penalty hearing on the grounds that trial counsel was ineffective for failing to investigate and to present mitigating evidence. Both appellant and the Commonwealth appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court stated that appellant had not shown that the alleged shortcomings of a county's indigent capital defense system in the mid-1990's were so extreme as to cause structural defects that tainted the entire mechanism of his trial and sentencing, so that the presumption of effectiveness was reversed. As for counsel's failure to argue that appellant was entitled to specific performance of a plea agreement, it was well within the District Attorney's discretion to condition the acceptance of appellant's guilty plea upon the agreement of his codefendant to take the plea agreement. Next, the PCRA court properly found that trial counsel was ineffective for failing to investigate and present mental health mitigation evidence. Trial counsel knew that appellant had been treated for mental disorders arising from appellant's sexual abuse; the murder occurred after the victim propositioned appellant for sex; and the mitigation evidence would have supported two mitigating factors not considered by the jury, 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711(e)(2) and (3). There was ample evidence that appellant was prejudiced by trial counsel's ineffectiveness, as the jury found no mitigating circumstances at all.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the PCRA court's decision.

Commonwealth v. Janssen Pharmaceutica, Inc.


No. 24 EAP 2009


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


607 Pa. 406; 8 A.3d 267; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 3051


October 21, 2009, Argued 
August 17, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County entered December 9, 2008 at 2181 January Term, 2008. Abramson, Howland W., Judge
Commonwealth v. Janssen Pharmaceutica, Inc., 2 A.3d 474, 2010 Pa. LEXIS 1805 (Pa., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee Office of General Counsel filed a tort action against appellant pharmaceutical company. The Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) denied appellant's motion to disqualify contingent fee counsel retained by appellee. The court granted appellant's application for extraordinary relief under 42 Pa.C.S. § 726.


OVERVIEW: The court stated that the threshold issue was whether appellant had standing under 71 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 732-103 to challenge counsel's representation of appellee. The court held that the language of § 732-103 was clear and unambiguous and thus clearly indicated the General Assembly's intent. The obvious interpretation of § 732-103 was that no party to an action, other than the Commonwealth agency involved in the action itself, could challenge the authority of the agency's legal representation. Because the statutory language was plain and unambiguous, the alternative construction offered by appellant had to fail. There was no merit to appellant's argument that the matter involved not a Commonwealth agency as plaintiff, but the Commonwealth itself. The action in fact was filed on behalf of two Commonwealth agencies. Although the parties did not advert to traditional standing principles, the limitation of § 732-103 aligned with those principles. It was difficult to see how a party-opponent in active litigation with the Commonwealth could be said to have a substantial, direct and immediate interest in the authority or identity of the legal representation the Commonwealth had chosen.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of, inter alia, first-degree murder of a police officer and multiple counts of reckless endangerment of another person (REAP). A death sentence and other consecutive terms of imprisonment were imposed on him.

There was no basis for the trial judge to recuse himself.

Tannenbaum v. Nationwide Ins. Co.


No. 100 MAP 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


605 Pa. 590; 992 A.2d 859; 2010 Pa. LEXIS 926


April 16, 2008, Argued 
April 28, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court No. 1269 EDA 2006 dated March 5, 2007 affirming the Bucks County Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division at No. 0404111-25-6 dated April 13, 2006. Appeal allowed: October 17, 2007 at 292 MAL 2007. Trial Court Judges: Rea Behney Boylan, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Correale F. Stevens, Seamus P. McCaffery, John T. Kelly, JJ.
Tannenbaum v. Nationwide Ins. Co., 2007 PA Super 54, 919 A.2d 267, 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 299 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee insured filed a petition against appellant insurer to set aside an arbitration award that implemented an offset under 75 Pa.C.S. § 1722 for disability payments the insured had received. The trial court vacated the award, and the Superior Court of Pennsylvania affirmed the trial court's decision. The insurer appealed.


OVERVIEW: Following an automobile accident, the insured received income-loss benefits under a group plan and benefits under two personal disability policies. Later, he sought to recover income-loss benefits under an underinsured motorist policy. The court held that the benefits the insured received under his group plan and personal disability policies fell within the group/program/arrangement classification for purposes of § 1722. There was no tenable basis to support limiting the scope of § 1722 to health benefits. Once it was accepted that the relevant income-loss benefits received by the insured fell within the group/program/arrangement classification, it became apparent that they were subject to the specified statutory offset. Section 1722 specifically directed that its prohibition and offset prescriptions applied relative to "any program, group contract or other arrangement for payment of benefits." Under the plain terms of § 1722, an insured's recovery under uninsured/underinsured policies could be offset by group/program/arrangement benefits, including disability benefits purchased in whole or in part by the insured, at least so long as those benefits were not subject to subrogation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the Superior Court.

In re Interbranch Comm'n on Juvenile Justice


No. 53 WM 2009


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

Application for Relief.
In re Lokuta, 989 A.2d 942, 2010 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 7 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2010)
Judicial Conduct Bd. v. Interbranch Comm'n on Juvenile Justice, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2395 (Pa., Nov. 6, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court exercised its King's Bench jurisdiction for the limited purpose of passing upon the question of confidentiality with regard to the pleading filed by petitioner, the Judicial Conduct Board of Pennsylvania (JCB), seeking relief from an order rejecting its objections to questions asked of its witnesses during a hearing conducted by respondent, the Interbranch Commission on Juvenile Justice (ICJJ)


OVERVIEW: The ICJJ was established to conduct a non-criminal investigation and review of the juvenile justice system in a particular county regarding the allegations of misconduct of two judges. The JCB's pleading requested that the Court issue an order precluding the ICJJ from compelling answers to the questions from any JCB witness who was called to testify, and from compelling the production of any documents pursuant to subpoenas or document requests. The JCB essentially asserted that the information and documents sought by the ICJJ were protected under the Pennsylvania Constitution because they relate to judicial conduct complaints where no formal charges have been filed and the subjects of the investigations have expressly declined to waive confidentiality. The Court interpreted Pa. Jud. Conduct Bd. R.P. 18 and held that Rule 18 did not preclude the JCB from disclosing matters relating to misconduct complaints filed against judges simply because no formal charges have yet been filed by the JCB. The Court noted that the ICJJ had assured the Court that it would maintain the confidentiality of the complaints it had requested.


OUTCOME: The Court directed the JCB to disclose the requested misconduct complaint under seal. The Court refused to order the disclosure of the ICJJ's requests for other investigative materials.

Commonwealth v. Housman


No. 452 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


604 Pa. 596; 986 A.2d 822; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2791


December 2, 2004, Argued; February 25, 2008, Re-Submitted 
December 29, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition denied by Housman v. Pennsylvania, 131 S. Ct. 199, 178 L. Ed. 2d 120, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 6808 (U.S., 2010)
Stay denied by, Stay lifted by, Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Without prejudice, Certificate of appealability denied Housman v. Wetzel, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 39054 (M.D. Pa., Mar. 22, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered on February 4, 2002 in the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, Criminal Division, at No. 01-246. Trial Court Judge: George E. Hoffer, Judge.
Commonwealth v. Markman, 591 Pa. 249, 916 A.2d 586, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 387 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a direct appeal, appellant challenged the death sentence imposed against him by the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), following his conviction for first degree murder, in violation of 18 Pa.C.S. § 2502(a), and the related crimes of kidnapping, theft by unlawful taking or disposition, unlawful restraint, abuse of corpse, and criminal conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted along with co-defendant for the murder of his girlfriend in an apparent love triangle. The court found that the evidence sufficiently established that appellant strangled the victim with speaker wire after restraining her movement. On appeal, appellant challenged the trial court's denial of his motion to sever his trial from co-defendant's. The court found no abuse of the trial court's discretion since neither denied that they murdered the victim, rather, they only disputed the roles each played in the murder. The fact that each blamed one another was insufficient to warrant separate trials based on antagonistic defenses. The court further found no violation of appellant's Confrontation Clause, U.S. Const. amend. VI, rights as a result of the admission of co-defendant's confession. Further, the court found that the sentence of death was not the product of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor since the evidence established that he strangled the victim. Further, the evidence supported the finding that at least one aggravating factor was proven, namely that appellant committed the killing while in the perpetration of a kidnapping, a felony.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence.

Commonwealth v. Smith


No. 500 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


604 Pa. 126; 985 A.2d 886; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2789


October 20, 2008, Argued 
December 29, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Smith v. Pennsylvania, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5789 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered on July 26, 2005 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. 0405-0297 2/2. Trial Court Judges: Shelia A. Woods-Skipper, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his sentence of death imposed by the Philadelphia Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) after a jury convicted him of first-degree murder, possession of an instrument of crime, reckless endangerment of another person, and criminal conspiracy. Defendant claimed, inter alia, that his convictions for first-degree murder and criminal conspiracy were not supported by sufficient evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's gang leader gave orders that any member of the rival gang should be shot on sight. A day or two later, a witness saw defendant and a codefendant begin firing shots down the street. The victim was shot and killed. Several months later defendant was observed throwing one of the guns used in the victim's murder into the street. His girlfriend admitted that he had her buy the gun two days before the victim was shot. Another witness testified that the codefendant had stated that he and defendant had shot someone in the face the night of the murder. The appellate court held the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's convictions for first-degree murder and conspiracy. The evidence at trial did not support a voluntary manslaughter charge. Defendant failed to offer any acceptable grounds for severance. The sentence of death was not a product of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor; rather, it was based on the evidence presented at trial. The sentence of death was not a product of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor, and the evidence supported the jury's finding of at least one aggravating circumstance.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence and convictions were affirmed.

City of Philadelphia v. FOP Lodge No. 5 (Breary)

SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

October 21, 2008, Argued 
December 29, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered on July 30, 2007 at No. 44 CD 2007, vacating and remanding the Order entered on December 16, 2006 in the Court of Common Pleas, of Philadelphia County, Civil Division at No. 4908 October Term 2006. Appeal allowed December 18, 2007 at 503 EAL 2007. Trial Court Judges: Gary S. Glazer, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Dan Pellegrini, Renee Jubelirer Cohn, Rober E. Simpson, JJ.
City of Philadelphia v. FOP Lodge No. 5, 932 A.2d 274, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 409 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant union challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which vacated and remanded a trial court order that denied respondent city's petition to vacate the arbitration award entered by an arbitrator, which sustained a grievance filed by respondent, a police union organization, and reinstated respondent grievant. An arbitration was conducted pursuant to Act 111, 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 217.1 - 217.10.


OVERVIEW: The grievant was a police officer who was alleged to have sexually assaulted another officer. Upon receiving notice that he was going to be terminated from his employment with the city's police department, a grievance was filed by the union on the grievant's behalf. The Commonwealth Court had held that the arbitrator violated the city's due process rights when the arbitrator precluded it from presenting any evidence because of its failure to comply with a duly issued subpoena. The court adopted the four factor test developed by the Superior Court of Pennsylvania as to what courts should examine to determine the severity and vitality of a discovery sanction and held that the Commonwealth Court correctly determined that the arbitrator, under the circumstances of the case, violated the procedural due process rights of the city. In particular, the court noted that, regarding the prongs of prejudice and willful misconduct, any prejudice was sufficiently cured by the city's subsequent actions and that no willful misconduct occurred on the part of the city.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.


 

155 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Fletcher


No. 545 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


604 Pa. 493; 986 A.2d 759; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2927


May 6, 2009, Submitted 
December 28, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. CP-51-CR-0360011-1992, dated April 26, 2007.
Commonwealth v. Fletcher, 586 Pa. 527, 896 A.2d 508, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 542 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate, who had been sentenced to death, sought relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act , 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9541-9546. The Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) dismissed the petition. Appellant challenged the trial court's decision.


OVERVIEW: The court stated that because appellant was granted the right to proceed pro se during the post-verdict phase, he could not successfully "layer" any claims under 42 Pa.C.S § 9543(a)(2)(ii) alleging trial counsel's ineffectiveness that he did not raise in a post-verdict motion. Review of such "layered" claims necessarily required an assessment of whether appellant himself was ineffective for not including the claims in a post-verdict motion. A defendant could not allege his own ineffectiveness or that of standby counsel. Even if counsel were ineffective under U.S. Const. amend. VI for stipulating to the introduction of an autopsy report wherein the doctor opined that the manner of death was homicide, appellant had not shown prejudice. Even in the absence of the stipulation, the Commonwealth would have been permitted to have another medical examiner review the doctor's factual findings and render an opinion as to the cause and manner of death. Moreover, "homicide" referred to any intentional killing of a human except for a suicide; the fact that counsel stipulated that the manner of death was homicide had no bearing on whether the killing was committed in self-defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying relief.

Fletcher v. Pa. Prop. & Cas. Ins. Guar. Ass'n


No. 41 MAP 2008


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


603 Pa. 452; 985 A.2d 678; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2631


December 4, 2008, Argued 
December 15, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Motion to strike denied by, Summary judgment granted, in part, summary judgment denied, in part by Fletcher v. Pa. Prop. & Cas. Ins. Guar. Ass'n & Pa., 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 405 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Aug. 26, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered on January 4, 2007, at No. 107 M.D. 2006 and amended on February 2, 2007 (42 Pa.C.S. sect. 702(b). Appeal allowed June 6, 2008 at 32 MM 2007. Intermediate Court Judges: Robert E. Simpson, Mary Hannah, Leavitt, JJ. James R. Kelley, Senior Judge.
Fletcher v. Pa. Prop. & Cas. Ins. Guar. Ass'n, 914 A.2d 477, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 3 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the Medical Care Availability and Reduction of Error Fund (MCARE Fund) and the Pennsylvania Property and Casualty Insurance Guaranty Association (PPCIGA), sought review of the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which determined that it had original jurisdiction over the declaratory judgment action brought by plaintiff, a successful medical malpractice litigant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff obtained a medical malpractice judgment in her favor for the death of her husband as a result of the medical malpractice of two doctors. The doctors' medical malpractice insurer became insolvent, and the PPCIGA assumed its obligations. Plaintiff asserted that the MCARE Fund was the excess carrier under the relevant policies and was responsible for excess liability up to $ 1.2 million for each of the doctors to the extent that the PPCIGA paid less than such amounts. The PPCIGA and the MCARE Fund denied coverage. The issue presented was whether a party challenging the MCARE Fund's coverage determination may bring an action in the Commonwealth Court's original jurisdiction or whether, as the MCARE Fund asserted, the challenging party must first exhaust administrative remedies by seeking relief with the Pennsylvania Insurance Department. The court held that the statutory changes to the Medical Care Availability and Reduction of Error Act, 40 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1303.101-1303.910, were too insignificant to compel it to abandon its prior case law decision that the Commonwealth Court had original jurisdiction over claims against the MCARE Fund.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and remanded the case to the Commonwealth Court for further proceedings.

Commonwealth v. Rompilla


No. 570 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


603 Pa. 332; 983 A.2d 1207; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2424


September 17, 2009, Submitted 
November 19, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Commonwealth v. Rompilla, 2010 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2351 (Pa. Super. Ct., May 18, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered on August 13, 2007 in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Lehigh County at No. CP-39-CR-0000682-1988. Trial Court Judge: Alan M. Black, Senior Judge.
Rompilla v. Beard, 545 U.S. 374, 125 S. Ct. 2456, 162 L. Ed. 2d 360, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 4846 (2005)



DISPOSITION: Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of a judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which imposed a sentence of imprisonment for his conviction of, inter alia, first-degree murder. The sentence was based on a sentencing agreement, and the trial court had denied post-sentence relief. The appeal had been transferred from a superior court.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted after a jury trial of first-degree murder and multiple other charges, arising from a fatal stabbing of a bar owner, and he was sentenced to death. His petition for relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9541-9546, was denied, but he was granted federal habeas corpus relief as to his penalty-phase claims. The matter was remanded to the trial court for a new penalty hearing. Prior to the rehearing, a sentencing agreement was reached which, inter alia, provided for a life sentence, and such sentences were imposed. However, the inmate challenged the judgment of sentence. The trial court dismissed the motion, finding that the inmate had waived his appellate rights in the agreement. The inmate appealed to the superior court, and the matter was transferred to the court. The court declined to review the appeal on the merits upon finding that the superior court was the proper forum to hear the matter. The sentence under review was life imprisonment, such that the court lacked jurisdiction pursuant to 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711. Rather, as no sentence of death was involved, the superior court had jurisdiction under 42 Pa.C.S. § 742.


OUTCOME: The court relinquished jurisdiction and transferred the appeal to the superior court.

Abrams v. Pneumo Abex Corp.


No. 17 EAP 2008, No. 18 EAP 2008


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


602 Pa. 627; 981 A.2d 198; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2212


October 20, 2008, Argued 
October 21, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on December 17, 2007 at No. 1182 EDA 2005, affirming the Judgment entered on April 4, 2005 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Civil Division at No. 3458 Feb. Term 2003. Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on December 17, 2007 at No. 1185 EDA 2005, affirming the Judgment entered on April 4, 2005 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Civil Division at No. 3459 Feb. Term 2003.
Abrams v. Pneumo Abex Corp., 2007 PA Super 383, 939 A.2d 388, 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4416 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administratrices challenged the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee company. Appellants each brought personal injury cases, which were consolidated, alleging that their husbands' lung cancer was caused by occupational exposure to asbestos-containing products manufactured by appellee. Appellants had been substituted as plaintiffs after their husbands died.


OVERVIEW: Appellants had brought suit against other companies for compensation for claims of nonmalignant asbestos-related disease. Appellants contended that their receipt of compensation from those other companies for claims which arose prior to 1992, while the one disease rule was in effect, did not preclude their subsequent recovery from appellee for damages for lung cancer that was diagnosed after the two disease rule was adopted in Pennsylvania. The court held that the lower court erred in its determination that appellants were precluded from seeking damages from appellee for lung cancer diagnosed after the two disease rule was adopted in the Commonwealth. The court held that case law set forth that the prior recovery of damages for increased risk and fear of developing cancer due to asbestos exposure, awarded under the one disease rule, did not preclude them from recovering, from a party he has not previously sued, damages for cancer that developed and was diagnosed after the separate disease rule, also referred to as the two disease rule.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order affirming the trial court's grant of the motion for summary judgment filed by the employer and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.


 

160 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Omar


No. 116 MAP 2007, No. 20 MAP 2008


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


602 Pa. 595; 981 A.2d 179; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2104; 63 A.L.R.6th 793; 95 U.S.P.Q.2D (BNA) 1921


December 2, 2008, Submitted, Argued 
October 5, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County dated November 15, 2007 at No. CP-14-CR-0001200-2007 Dismissing Count 1 as Unconstitutional and Quashing the Criminal Information. Appeal from the Order of the Centre County Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, dated January 25, 2008 at No. CP-14-CR-2020-2007.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In these consolidated cases, the Centre County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) dismissed charges filed by the Commonwealth for violations of the Pennsylvania Trademark Counterfeiting Statute, 18 Pa.C.S. § 4119, based on the trial court's prior decision striking the statute as unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. The Commonwealth appealed.


OVERVIEW: The state's highest court held under § 4119, the use of any items bearing an unauthorized reproduction of terms or words used by a person to identify that person's goods or services was criminalized. Thus, § 4119(a) and (i) unconstitutionally prohibited protected speech, including the use of words on a sign praising or protesting any entity with a trademarked name. The Commonwealth's attempt to apply the limiting phrase "with intent to sell or distribute" to the entire list of verbs in the definition of the offense in § 4119(a) was rejected. The amendment would have applied solely to "possessed." Further, the constitutionality of the proposed amendatory language could not be considered until it was enacted into law. A default mens rea in 18 Pa.C.S. § 302(c) did not speak to what conduct a person had to commit, and did not mention the intent to sell or deceive. 18 Pa.C.S. § 312 did not save § 4119 as the concern of overbreadth jurisprudence was not only at the risk of prosecution, but also the chilling of speech in fear of prosecution. The statutes of other states cited by the Commonwealth were distinguished. Section 4119 was properly stricken as overbroad.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Chambers


No. 489 CAP


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


602 Pa. 224; 980 A.2d 35; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2039


October 20, 2008, Argued 
September 30, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Chambers v. Pennsylvania, 560 U.S. 928, 130 S. Ct. 3323, 176 L. Ed. 2d 1226, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 4307 (2010)
Related proceeding at Chambers v. Phila. Media Network, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 54406 (E.D. Pa., Apr. 15, 2013)
Subsequent civil proceeding at Chambers v. Hughes, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 57025 (E.D. Pa., Apr. 19, 2013)
Related proceeding at J.C. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare, 2016 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 206 (2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence entered on May 26, 2005 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. 03-11-0142 1/3.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him to death for the first-degree murder of a three-year-old girl, in violation of 18 Pa.C.S. § 2502, and other related crimes. The jury found two aggravating factors, namely that the victim was under the age of 12 when she was murdered and torture.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's convictions arose from the death of the little girl, whom he was paid to care for, along with her siblings. The Court found that the totality of the evidence amply proved specific intent to kill beyond a reasonable doubt based on the evidence that showed not only that defendant inflicted a specific blow to the child, but immediate prior abuse, a failure to so much as check on the child after she fell into a radiator after being hit by defendant and slowly suffocated, and defendant forbidding the victim's sister from going to her assistance. The Court also found that the evidence was sufficient to support the jury's finding of the torture aggravator based on the beatings and neglect defendant inflicted upon the victim taking place over the course of months; her suffering sudden and serious weight loss; the months of torture then culminating on the night of the killing, as defendant beat her with an extension cord in a cold shower, then beat her again later that evening, just before throwing her into the radiator with sufficient force to leave indentations in her face and head; then throwing her behind a bed, where she slowly suffocated over the course of several hours.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions and judgment of sentence.

Commonwealth v. Santiago


No. 52 EAP 2008


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


602 Pa. 159; 978 A.2d 349; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1784


March 20, 2009, Submitted 
August 25, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered at No. 2471 EDA 2006 on May 2, 2008.
Commonwealth v. Santiago, 953 A.2d 839, 2008 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1643 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court assumed plenary jurisdiction to review the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which found that court-appointed appellate counsel's Anders brief, seeking withdraw from representation on appeal, failed to meet the requirements of Anders and other case law. Appellant was convicted on charges involving possession of drugs and criminal conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Appellate counsel had filed a petition to withdraw from representation and an accompanying Anders brief with the Superior Court setting forth the factual history of the entire case and concluding that the appeal was wholly frivolous as there were no meritorious issues to present. The court agreed with the Superior Court that the brief was inadequate, but on different grounds. Namely, the Court found that appellate counsel did not summarize his own review of the record with an eye toward possible appellate issues but, instead, identified two challenges to the sufficiency of the evidence that appellant himself had asked appellate counsel to raise. The Court adjusted its rules prospectively regarding the contents of an Anders brief in Pennsylvania. The court held that in appeals where the briefing notice was issued after the date of the decision in the case at bar, counsel who seek to withdraw on grounds that their clients' appeals are frivolous shall set forth the reasons for that conclusion in the Anders brief.


OUTCOME: The Court affirmed the order of the Superior Court, on different grounds, that appellate counsel's brief was inadequate. The court remanded the case to the Superior Court for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion. The court directed that if appellate counsel filed another brief, said brief was to conform to the requirements set forth in the Court's opinion.

Spahn v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment


Nos. 25 & 26 EAP 2008, No. 27 EAP 2008, No. 28 EAP 2008


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


602 Pa. 83; 977 A.2d 1132; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1696


December 2, 2008, Argued 
August 18, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the order of the Commonwealth Court entered on April 4, 2007 at No. 1425 & 1426 CD 2006 (reargument denied on 05/30/2007) affirming the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, dated July 5, 2006 at No. 0111 September Term 2005. 922 A.2d 24 (Pa. Commw. 2007).

Appeal from the order of the Commonwealth Court, dated April 4, 2007 at No. 1528 CD 2006 (reargument denied on 05/25/2007) affirming the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, dated July 13, 2006 at No. 1048 November Term 2005. 921 A.2d 536 (Pa. Commw. 2007).

Appeal from the order of the Commonwealth Court, dated April 9, 2008 at No. 1609 CD 2007, affirming the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, dated August 8, 2007 at No. 003362, December Term 2006. 951 A.2d 398 (Pa. Commw. 2008).
Soc'y Created to Reduce Urban Blight v. Zoning Hearing Bd. of Adjustment, 951 A.2d 398, 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 310 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2008)
Spahn v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment, 922 A.2d 24, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 159 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2007)
Soc'y Created to Reduce Urban Blight v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment, 921 A.2d 536, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 135 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, appellant taxpayers challenged the orders of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which determined that their standing in the respective zoning appeals was removed by the enactment of the Home Rule Act, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 13131.1. Appellees, a city, its zoning board, and various entities were parties to the suits that were consolidated since each concerned the interpretation and constitutionality of § 13131.1.


OVERVIEW: Each case involved a general taxpayer challenging the action of the city's zoning board of adjustment granting a use and/or zoning variance to the various entities, who had applied for the same. The various entities challenged the taxpayers' appeals on the basis that they were not aggrieved persons under § 13131.1. The taxpayers asserted aggrieved party standing under a city ordinance granting them the same. The court found that it was clear that the city's ordinance granting general taxpayer standing must cede to the plain language of § 13131.1. The court found that § 13131.1 clearly defined the parties who have standing to appeal the city's zoning decisions and it specifically excluded taxpayers who are not aggrieved by the decision. The Court held that the provision was directly contrary to the city's zoning code, which granted broader taxpayer standing in zoning appeals, thus, § 13131.1 took precedence and controlled. As a result, the orders of the Commonwealth Court properly determined that the taxpayers were not aggrieved parties in their respective challenges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the Commonwealth Court.

Moody v. Allegheny Valley Land Trust


No. 22 WAP 2008


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


601 Pa. 655; 976 A.2d 484; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1328


September 8, 2008, Argued 
July 22, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Moody v. Allegheny Valley Land, 176 L. Ed. 2d 110, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 1219 (U.S., Feb. 22, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered June 25, 2007 at No. 914 WDA 2006, reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Armstrong County entered December 30, 2005 at No. 1995-0963 and remanding. 2007 PA Super 189, 930 A.2d 505 (Pa. Super. 2007).
Moody v. Allegheny Valley Land Trust, 2007 PA Super 189, 930 A.2d 505, 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1718 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, landowners of servient estates, sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) which reversed the trial court's finding that a railroad right-of-way had been abandoned and that the servient estates were therefore no longer subject to an easement where the right-of-way had been transferred to appellee "rails-to-trails" organization.


OVERVIEW: The former holder of the contested right-of-way obtained permission from the Interstate Commerce Commission to abandon rail service on the right-of-way, after which it entered into an agreement with appellee Conservancy to convey the right-of-way to an organization nominated by the Conservancy. The Conservancy nominated the rails-to-trails organization to receive the right-of-way. The right-of-way was conveyed by quitclaim deed. The former holder of the railway turned over responsibility for maintenance of road crossings and bridges on the right-of-way to the rails to trails organization, the Conservancy, and the County. The essence of the dispute was whether an effort to railbank a railroad right-of-way via the private railbanking process could be effective if the railroad did not agree to be bound to resuscitate service if directed to do so at a later date. The appellate court held that the manner of railbanking at issue was effective and a railbanking did result. Accordingly, the landowners' claim that the right-of-way was abandoned, and the easement over their land extinguished, was without merit.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower appellate court was affirmed.


 

166 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Commonwealth v. McCoy


No. 48 MAP 2008


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


601 Pa. 540; 975 A.2d 586; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1323


December 2, 2008, Argued 
July 21, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of Superior Court at No. 670 MDA 2005 dated 02/21/2006 affirming the order of Dauphin County Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, at No. 1808-CR-2004 dated 03-21-2005.
Commonwealth v. McCoy, 2006 PA Super 33, 895 A.2d 18, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 108 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two counts of driving under the influence (DUI) in violation of 75 Pa.C.S. § 3802. He appealed, arguing that § 3802 was unconstitutional. The Pennsylvania Superior Court affirmed. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant lost control of his vehicle and struck a guard rail. When officers arrived, they observed he smelled of alcohol, had glassy, bloodshot eyes, and slurred speech. He was arrested; a blood test revealed that his blood alcohol content (BAC) was 0.233 percent. He argued that the BAC test constituted a "critical stage" at which the right to counsel attached, and that he was denied that right. The issue was whether U.S. Const. amend. VI and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9 provided a right to counsel before refusing to submit to chemical testing when such refusal would subject the DUI suspect to imprisonment pursuant to 75 Pa.C.S. § 3804(c). The high court held that defendant's circumstances did not trigger the right to counsel because, although he was under arrest, no criminal complaint had been filed, and the adversarial judicial proceedings had yet to begin. As this was not a "critical stage" under Pennsylvania's jurisprudence, the right did not attach.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

Stilp v. Commonwealth


No. 81 MAP 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


601 Pa. 429; 974 A.2d 491; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1319


January 28, 2008, Submitted 
July 20, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Orders of the Commonwealth Court dated November 13, 2006 and July 11, 2007 at No. 1 M.D. 2006. 910 A.2d 775 (Pa. Cmwlth. 2006). 929 A.2d 660 (Pa. Cmwlth. 2007).
Stilp v. Commonwealth, 929 A.2d 660, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 379 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2007)
Stilp v. Commonwealth, 910 A.2d 775, 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 604 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizen filed a petition for review against appellees, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, its General Assembly, and the General Assembly's leadership, claiming that certain items of value received by legislators violated Pa. Const. art. II, § 8 and art. VII, § 14. The Pennsylvania Commonwealth Court dismissed 13 counts and granted appellees' application for judgment on the pleadings on the remaining count. Appellant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Art. II, § 8 provided that a legislator could not receive any increase of salary or mileage under any law passed during the term for which he or she was elected. The trial court held that none of appellant's challenges stated a claim for relief. The high court agreed. Cost of living adjustments received by legislators pursuant to 65 Pa. Stat. Ann § 366.4 were "salary" for purposes of art. II, § 8 and were fixed and predetermined in the Pennsylvania Compensation Law. The expenses received by legislators that appellant complained of were authorized by § 366.4(a), and reimbursing them for such expenses, even if they were unvouchered, was constitutional. The legislators' insurance, pension, and other benefits did not fall within the prohibition expressed by art. I, § 8. The high court rejected appellant's claim that accumulations in the leadership accounts violated Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 14, which required surplus operating funds to be appropriated during the next fiscal year. As these "continuing appropriations" were committed and did not lapse at the close of the fiscal year, they were not a "surplus" that was available for appropriation during the next fiscal year.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of appellant's petition for review.


 

168 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Diodoro


No. 117 MAP 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


601 Pa. 6; 970 A.2d 1100; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 946


May 14, 2008, Argued 
May 26, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Diodoro v. Pennsylvania, 130 S. Ct. 200, 175 L. Ed. 2d 127, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 5967 (U.S., Oct. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the order of the Superior Court, entered August 23, 2007, at No. 1889 EDA 2005, affirming the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, entered May 23, 2005, at No. 2200-04. 2007 PA Super 256, 932 A.2d 172 (Pa. Super. 2007).
Commonwealth v. Diodoro, 2007 PA Super 256, 932 A.2d 172, 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2690 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed his conviction for sexual abuse of children in violation of 18 Pa.C.S. § 6312(d). He argued that the superior court erred in finding that he had control of child pornography for proposes of § 6312(d)(1).


OVERVIEW: Police obtained a search warrant for defendant's personal computer and seized the computer from his residence. The computer contained images of suspected child pornography and images that were known to be child pornography. A jury convicted defendant of violating § 6312(d). The supreme court held that accessing and viewing child pornography over the internet constituted "control" of such pornography under § 6312(d). Under § 6312(d), a defendant could be convicted of sexual abuse of children for the mere knowing control of child pornography. An individual manifested knowing control of child pornography when he purposefully searched it out on the internet and intentionally viewed it on his computer. Under the statute, the Commonwealth did not have to show that a defendant possessed child pornography to prove a violation of § 6312(d) if it could prove that a defendant knowingly controlled child pornography. Section 6312(d)(1) employed the disjunctive "knowingly possessed or controlled." Therefore, it was reasonable to conclude that the General Assembly employed the terms "possession" and "control" in the disjunctive purposefully and that they were meant to have different applications.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the order of the superior court.

 

169 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



DePaul v. Commonwealth


No. 194 EM 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


600 Pa. 573; 969 A.2d 536; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 670


May 13, 2008, Argued 
April 30, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at DePaul v. Commonwealth, 2012 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 886 (2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Riverwalk Casino v. Pa. Gaming Control Bd., 592 Pa. 505, 926 A.2d 926, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1430 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, who held an ownership interest in a casino, filed an action for declaratory judgment against respondents, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and the Pennsylvania Gaming Control Board, in which he argued in part that 4 Pa.C.S. § 1513, part of the Pennsylvania Race Horse Development and Gaming Act, violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 7.


OVERVIEW: Section 1513 imposed upon a class of individuals affiliated with licensed gaming in Pennsylvania an absolute ban on political contributions. The court first noted that the Commonwealth did not dispute that the appropriate constitutional test was strict scrutiny. Furthermore, for purposes of § 7, political contributions were a form of nonverbal, protected expression. While the ban on political contributions here did further the compelling state interest in avoiding the appearance of corruption in the oversight of the gaming industry, however, § 1513 was not narrowly tailored. Banning all contributions was not a narrowly drawn means of furthering a policy of negating the corrupting effect and appearance of large contributions. It totally banned a protected form of political expression and association which was unrelated to the identified interest, and did so despite the availability of more narrowly tailored restrictions. The obvious disconnection between the articulated interest and the means chosen rendered the absolute ban on campaign contributions constitutionally infirm under § 7.


OUTCOME: The court held that § 1513 was unconstitutional under § 7. It enjoined its enforcement.

Clifton v. Allegheny County


No. 20 WAP 2007, No. 21 WAP 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


600 Pa. 662; 969 A.2d 1197; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 669


September 10, 2008, Argued 
April 29, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Application denied by Clifton v. Allegheny County, 980 A.2d 27, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1614 (Pa., Aug. 7, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, entered July 25, 2007 at No. GD-05-028638. Appeal from the Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County entered July 25, 2007 at No GD-05-028355.
Clifton v. Allegheny County, 2007 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 202 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Allegheny County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), in a case where appellee property owners sued appellant county over use of a base-year property tax assessment system the owners believed caused property taxes to be assessed in the county in violation of Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1's Uniformity Clause, entered a judgment that found the system was facially unconstitutional. The county appealed.


OVERVIEW: A trial court ultimately ordered in a property tax case that a comprehensive countywide assessment be completed by a certain time for use in an upcoming tax year. The county performed an annual reassessment. An implementing ordinance was then amended to provide that the assessment for that year would be used as a base-year to calculate property taxes in the future. No reassessment occurred. The owners sued to challenge use of the base-year formula to calculate the property tax assessment and claimed it violated the Uniformity Clause of the Pennsylvania Constitution, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1. The trial court found that the base-year assessment system was facially unconstitutional and that the county had to reassess property on an annual basis. The state supreme court found that the base-year assessment system was not facially unconstitutional, but was unconstitutional as applied because it did not consider real estate valuation changes, which meant that the property valuations were less accurate as time passed. It also found that the trial court would need on remand to determine how often the reassessments should occur to make property valuations constitutionally uniform.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court agreed that the current base-year assessment system of property taxation was unconstitutional, but disagreed that it was facially unconstitutional; rather, it found more narrowly that it was unconstitutional as applied because use of a base-year caused inaccuracies and a lack of uniformity over time. It then found reassessment was required and remanded the case to the trial court to determine how it should be accomplished.

Yount v. Pa. Dep't of Corr.


No. 97 MAP 2006


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


600 Pa. 418; 966 A.2d 1115; 2009 Pa. LEXIS 480


March 4, 2008, Argued; August 14, 2008, Re-Submitted 
March 20, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered on 7/19/06 at No. 305 M.D. 2005.
Yount v. Pa. Dep't of Corr., 886 A.2d 1163, 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 690 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate filed a petition for review in the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, alleging that his transfer from one prison facility to another prison facility by appellees, the Pennsylvania Department of Corrections and its superintendent, in his official capacity, was done in retaliation. The court granted summary judgment to the Department and its superintendent on the retaliatory transfer claim. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The inmate escaped from a prison detail with the cooperation from a prison volunteer. After the inmate was recaptured, he was incarcerated at a prison. While there, the inmate and other prisoners filed a lawsuit, alleging that the prison's telephone service provider overcharged them. While the suit was pending, the inmate held private conversations with a different prison volunteer, who revealed personal information. Upon learning of these events, the Department recommended the inmate's transfer, noting that his previous escape was consummated with the assistance of a prison volunteer, and reasoning the developing close relationship with another volunteer created an escape risk. The inmate was transferred to another prison. On appeal, the court found that administrative prisoner transfers were presumed to further a legitimate penological objective unless a prisoner proved otherwise. Further, there was highly relevant temporal proximity between the events constituting the Department's grounds for the transfer and the decision to transfer the inmate. Thus, the inmate failed to demonstrate that his transfer was due to retaliation by prison officials for his institution of the lawsuit.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Walter

SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

Habeas corpus proceeding at, Request granted, Motion granted by Walter v. Beard, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 23701 (M.D. Pa., Mar. 15, 2010)
Decision reached on appeal by Commonwealth v. Walter, 2015 Pa. LEXIS 1542 (Pa., July 20, 2015)

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Court of Common Pleas of Clinton County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), convicted defendant of first-degree murder and felony theft. The jury returned a death sentence, and the trial court imposed the death sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth gave notice of its intent to seek the death penalty, based on a single aggravating circumstance: murder committed while in the perpetration of a felony. Defendant argued that the trial court erred in failing to dismiss the aggravating circumstance. OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. McMullen

Appeal after remand at Commonwealth v. McMullen, 984 A.2d 1018, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4842 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Judgment of Superior Court entered on 7/6/2005 at No. 2899 EDA 2003 (reargument denied on 9/14/2005) reversing, vacating and remanding the Judgment of Sentence entered on 8/20/2003 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Criminal Division at No. CP-51-CR-0606701-2001. Appeal from the Judgment of Superior Court entered on 7/6/2005 at No. 1271 EDA 2004 (reargument denied on 9/14/2005) reversing, vacating and remanding the Judgment of Sentence entered on 8/20/2003 in the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Criminal Division at Nos. MC-51-CR-0824791-2003 and CP-51-CR-0606701-2001.
Commonwealth v. McMullen, 2005 PA Super 248, 881 A.2d 841, 2005 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1597 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed, vacated, and remanded a judgment of sentence entered in two cases finding defendant in indirect criminal contempt.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had pleaded guilty to, inter alia, stalking and harassment by communication and had been granted immediate parole to passive house arrest with orders not to contact the victim. Defendant thereafter left the jurisdiction without permission and began calling the victim from Florida. The trial court revoked defendant's probation and sentenced him, inter alia, to 2 1/2 to 5 years in prison for stalking with a consecutive 6 to 12 month term for harassment by communication. The superior court vacated and remanded. On further appeal, the court reversed and remanded. Const. art. V, § 10(c) and the courts had the inherent authority to enforce their orders by way of the power of contempt.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's order and remanded the case to the superior court.

Pa. Bankers Ass'n v. Pa. Dep't of Banking


No. 44 MAP 2007, No. 45 MAP 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


599 Pa. 496; 962 A.2d 609; 2008 Pa. LEXIS 2261


April 16, 2008, Argued 
December 17, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered November 13, 2006 at 397 M.D. 2005 vacating and remanding the decision of the Department of Banking dated July 8, 2005 at MISC-2005-01. Appeal allowed April 26, 2007 at 40 MAL 2007. Intermediate Court Judges: Rochelle S. Friedman, Renee Cohn Jubelirer, JJ. Joseph F. McCloskey, Senior Judge.
Pa. Bankers Ass'n v. Pa. Dep't of Banking, 910 A.2d 767, 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 605 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) vacated the Department of Banking's approval of a credit union's proposal to charter itself as a community-based credit union, because the Department did not comply with 10 Pa. Code §§ 3.6, 3.4(b), hold evidentiary hearings, or provide protesting banks access to the hearing file. The credit union and the Department appealed.


OVERVIEW: Appellants claimed that 10 Pa. Code ch. 3 did not apply because credit unions were not 10 Pa. Code § 3.1 institutions and that they could not file a 10 Pa. Code § 3.2(b) application. The court agreed. 71 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 733-2(A) defined the term "institution" as a corporation, person, or mutual holding company, but not credit unions unless specifically stated otherwise. 7 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 102 excluded credit unions from the definition of "institution." Under either of these definitions, a credit union was not an "institution" (within the meaning of the Department's regulations) and could not have filed an "application," thereby removing the case from the scope of 10 Pa. Code ch. 3. Although the banks complied with 17 Pa.C.S. § 501(g) by filing a timely notice of protest, the protest was fatally flawed because it was filed pursuant to 10 Pa. Code ch. 3. The banks waived any due process right to a hearing pursuant to 2 Pa.C.S. § 504 because the issue was not timely raised. The holding in Conestoga did not apply because the banks did not establish standing. The court held that it was error to declare 71 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 733-302 unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the commonwealth court and remanded the cases for further proceedings consistent with the opinion and to address the alternative constitutional argument that the non-disclosure provision impermissibly restricted the banks' right to engage in public debate.


 

175 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Chase


No. 18 WAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


599 Pa. 80; 960 A.2d 108; 2008 Pa. LEXIS 2180


September 13, 2006, Argued 
November 26, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County entered on April 20, 2005 at No. CP-25-CR-0003520-2004.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with driving under the influence and two traffic offenses. Defendant's suppression motion was granted by the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which concluded that the Fourth Amendment and Pa. Const. art. I, § 8 required probable cause to stop a vehicle for an alleged traffic violation, and that 75 Pa.C.S. § 6308 was unconstitutional. The Commonwealth appealed.


OVERVIEW: The state's highest court held that Terry stops based on reasonable suspicion were allowed to secure such other information as a police officer reasonably believed to be necessary to enforce the provisions of § 6308(b). A modification of § 6308 merely eliminated the statutory requirement of a greater level of information for a stop under the Pennsylvania Motor Vehicle Code than was constitutionally required for all other stops. Section 6308(b) was not unconstitutional under the Fourth Amendment. The text of Pa. Const. art. I, § 8 did not require probable cause to make a vehicle stop. Since the Fourth Amendment and § 8 were coterminous for Terry purposes, and the Fourth Amendment allowed for an investigative detention in the form of a vehicle stop, § 8 allowed for a vehicle stop based on reasonable suspicion. Prior caselaw did not hold that § 8 required a vehicle stop to be supported by probable cause. The Pennsylvania legislature determined the reasonable suspicion standard adequately balanced citizens' privacy in a vehicle and law enforcement's ability to briefly investigate an alleged violation of the Code. Section 6308(b) was constitutional under the Pennsylvania Constitution.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed. The case was remanded to the trial court to determine if the police officer had reasonable suspicion to stop defendant's vehicle.

Appeal from the Judgments of Sentence of Death entered May 12, 2001, and the Judgment of Sentence entered September 6, 2001 (post-sentence motions denied on December 29, 2005), in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County at Nos. CC 200014712, 200014713, and 200014714.
Donegal Mut. Ins. Co. v. Baumhammers, 595 Pa. 147, 938 A.2d 286, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2886 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of five counts of first-degree murder and similar crimes. The jury found returned sentences of death as mandated by law, 42 Pa.C.S. § 9711(c)(iv). Smolow v. Hafer


No. 32 MAP 2005


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


598 Pa. 561; 959 A.2d 298; 2008 Pa. LEXIS 2049


March 5, 2008, Argued 
November 19, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered on February 9, 2005 at No. 208 MD 2004. 867 A.2d 767.
Smolow v. Hafer, 867 A.2d 767, 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 56 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner filed a class action against appellees, the state agency and its administrator, in the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, to challenge the constitutionality of Pennsylvania's Disposition of Abandoned and Unclaimed Property Act of 1982 (DAUPA), 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 1301.1 - 1301.29, and to recover damages and attorney fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The court dismissed the complaint. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The state agency took possession of shares of common stock in a company as abandoned and/or unclaimed property, pursuant to the DAUPA, 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. §1301.6 (amended 2002). The agency later sold this stock, under authority provided in 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1301.17, and appropriated the money, earned interest, and other appreciation for public purposes pursuant to 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1301.18. The owner filed a claim with the agency asserting ownership of the stock and seeking recovery of its proceeds. The claim was granted. The owner asserted that the state agency committed a taking without just compensation, under Pa. Const. art. I, § 10, when it denied the owner's request for interest earned during the time the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania held the owner's property pursuant to the DAUPA. On appeal, the court found that the owner had no claim to the interest earned while the abandoned property was held in perpetual temporary custody of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania. The DAUPA procedures resulted not from a taking by overt act of the Commonwealth, but were triggered by the neglect of the owner.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of the compliant was affirmed.


 

178 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Worthy


No. 1 WAP 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


598 Pa. 470; 957 A.2d 720; 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1905


September 10, 2007, Argued; January 14, 2008, Resubmitted 
October 27, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered June 29, 2006 at No. 1877 WDA 2004, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County entered October 19, 2004, at CP-02-CR-0012387-2002. Appeal allowed January 9, 2007 at 383 WAL 2006. Trial Court Judge: Lester G. Nauhaus, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Kate Ford Elliott, President Judge, Debra Todd, Zoran Popovich, JJ. 2006 PA Super 160, 903 A.2d 576 (Pa.Super. 2006).
Commonwealth v. Worthy, 2006 PA Super 160, 903 A.2d 576, 2006 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1545 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth challenged an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the decision of a trial court to grant defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained during a stop at a sobriety checkpoint. Defendant argued that his rights under the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution and Pa. Const. art. I, § 8 were violated because the checkpoint was not conducted in accordance with the Tarbert-Blouse guidelines.


OVERVIEW: A sergeant and police officers established a sobriety checkpoint. An officer stopped defendant's vehicle at the checkpoint and administered field sobriety tests upon him. Defendant failed the tests and was charged with two driving under the influence offenses. The supreme court held that the procedure used did not violate the Tarbert-Blouse proscription against placing unfettered discretion with the on-site officers as to which vehicles to stop at the checkpoint. On-site police officers could exercise their discretion to suspend temporarily the operation of a sobriety checkpoint because of traffic back-up that created unreasonable delay or safety concerns. When the checkpoint was in operation every vehicle was stopped to allow the officers to briefly observe the driver. Only when the traffic volume became heavy and the resulting back-up caused an unreasonable delay did the sergeant temporarily suspend operation of the checkpoint and allow every vehicle to pass through unimpeded. There was no allegation that the officers based the decision as to when to suspend or when to resume operation of the checkpoint on the characteristics or actions of any particular vehicle or driver.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the order and remanded the case to the trial court for proceedings consistent with the opinion.

Commonwealth v. Leidig


No. 17 MAP 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


598 Pa. 211; 956 A.2d 399; 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1546


May 12, 2008, Argued 
September 24, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered May 14, 2004, at No. 442 MDA 2003, 2004 PA Super 167, 850 A.2d 743 (Pa.Super. 2004), affirming the Judgment of Sentence of the court of Common Pleas of Franklin County, Criminal Division entered January 22, 2003 at No. 70 of 2002. Appeal allowed February 28,2007 at 527 MAL 2004. Trial Court Judge: John R. Walker, President Judge. Trial Court Judges: Kate Ford Elliott, President Judge. Debra Todd, Zoran Popovich, JJ.
Commonwealth v. Leidig, 2004 PA Super 167, 850 A.2d 743, 2004 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1205 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court, affirming the judgment of sentence of the court of Common Pleas of Franklin County, following a plea of nolo contender to a sexual offender charge. The Superior Court determined that defendant was not entitled to withdraw his plea even though he and the trial court might have been under a mistaken impression as to the length defendant's registration obligation under Megan's law.


OVERVIEW: After defendant entered a plea of nolo contendere to a sexual offender charge, he was informed that under the version of Megan's Law in effect at the time the crime was committed, he would be subject to registration as a sexual offender for ten years. However, the Superior Court determined that the lifetime registration requirement of the current version of Megan's Law, 42 Pa.C.S. § 9795.1(b)(2) applied. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the Megan's Law registration requirements, of whatever duration, were matters collateral to and not direct consequences of defendant's plea. The registration requirements were not a criminal penalty and were a civil requirement over which the sentencing judge had no control. 42 Pa.C.S. § 9795.3 plainly stated that the sentencing court, not the trial court accepting a plea, was the adjudicatory body that was to inform the sexual offender of the duty to register. Thus, the trial court was not obligated to advise defendant as to the length of the registration requirement, and any misunderstanding as to the duration of that requirement was not a basis for a post-sentence withdrawal of defendant's plea.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Superior Court's order.

Castellani v. Scranton Times, L.P.


No. 60 MAP 2007


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


598 Pa. 283; 956 A.2d 937; 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1547; 36 Media L. Rep. 2460


April 16, 2008, Argued 
September 24, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court at No. 1111 MDA 2005 entered January 3, 2007, reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County, Civil Division at No. 05-CV-69 dated June 3, 2005. Appeal allowed June 6, 2007 at 117 MAL 2007. Trial Court Judge Robert A. Mazzoni, Judge. Intermediate Court Judges: Maureen Lally-Green, Debra Todd, Zoran Popovich, JJ. 2007 PA Super 2, 916 A.2d 648 (Pa. Super. 2007).
Castellani v. Scranton Times, L.P., 2007 PA Super 2, 916 A.2d 648, 2007 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, former county elected officials, challenged a decision of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which, pursuant to Pennsylvania's Shield Law, 42 Pa.C.S. § 5942, reversed the trial court's order compelling appellees, two newspapers and a reporter, to disclose a confidential source.


OVERVIEW: The officials testified before a grand jury. The newspapers published front-page stories accusing the officials of "stonewalling" the grand jury. The articles attributed information to an unnamed source close to the investigation. The officials sued appellees for defamation and sought disclosure of the unnamed source. The trial court granted the request, finding that the Shield Law had to give way to the need to enforce and protect the foundation of the purpose of the grand jury. The superior court reversed. On appeal, the officials argued that the Shield Law should not protect appellees from disclosure because the communication between the reporter and her alleged source violated the Grand Jury Act, 42 Pa.C.S. § 4549(b), and thus constituted a criminal act. In affirming the superior court's decision, the court rejected the invitation to fashion a non-textual, crime-fraud exception to the operation of the Shield Law because such an exception would not only contradict the well-established public policy underlying the Shield Law, but would contravene the Shield Law's unambiguous text.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's decision.


 

181 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Jubelirer v. Rendell


No. 102 MAP 2006


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


598 Pa. 16; 953 A.2d 514; 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1332


March 4, 2008, Argued 
August 19, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated August 10, 2006 at No. 490MD2005 granting Summary Relief in favor of appellees. Trial Court Judges: James Gardner Colins, Commonwealth Court Senior Judge. Bernard L. McGinley, Dan Pellegrini, Rochelle S. Friedman and Robert E. Simpson, JJ. 904 A.2d 1030 (Pa. Cmwlth. 2006).
Jubelirer v. Rendell, 904 A.2d 1030, 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 440 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a former state senator and representative, sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which granted summary relief under Pa.R.A.P. 1532(b) to appellees, the governor and certain member of his administration, in appellants' action seeking a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief regarding gubernatorial vetoes of §§ 219, 223, 801, and 2010 of the General Appropriation Act of 2005.


OVERVIEW: Appellants claimed that the challenged vetoes were null and void as Pa. Const. art. IV, § 16 allegedly did not permit the governor to disapprove of language -- as opposed to amounts appropriated -- in the appropriation bill. The lower court determined that prior case law indicated that § 16 permitted the governor to disapprove of either the amount of an appropriation or the purpose for which the money was appropriated or both. On appeal, the court reversed in part and affirmed in part. The court focused on the meaning of the term "item" in § 16. Utilizing Pa. Const. art. IV, § 15 as the context, the phrase "item(s) of any bill making appropriations of money" was used to distinguish the type of bill to which the constitution was referring in comparison with § 15, which involved general legislation. Thus, the court concluded that "item" in § 16 was a sum of money directed by the General Assembly to be spent for a particular purpose. Based on that conclusion, because § 2010 did not include a subject connected to an amount and §§ 219 and 223 involved restrictions on the discretion of state agencies, those sections were improperly vetoed by the governor.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order to the extent it upheld the vetoes of language in §§ 219, 223, and 2010 of the Act. The court affirmed that part of the order that upheld the veto of the language in § 801.
